Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
7 min
The Definition of Nikah: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Views
Opening commentary on the Book of Marriage defining nikah and its legal meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the legal meaning of nikah and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Nikah is legally real in intercourse, not the contract — a dispute that affects oaths and legal presumptions.

The Hanafi school holds that the word nikah primarily refers to sexual intercourse, while the contract is a metaphorical extension. This position differs from Imam al-Shafi'i, who sees the contract as the primary meaning. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind the Hanafi view and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate in the passage where the author states 'al-sahih 'inda ashabina' (the correct view according to our scholars). Compare that to the view attributed to al-Shafi'i.
The Hanafi position is that nikah is a reality in intercourse; Shafi'i says reality in contract.
2
Understand the linguistic basis
Recognize that the passage mentions the linguistic meaning (al-damm wa al-jam') and that one of its specific uses is intercourse. The Hanafi school builds on this linguistic foundation.
Linguistic to legal: the shari'ah does not create entirely new meanings unless evidence proves otherwise.
3
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a person swears an oath not to marry, under the Hanafi definition, intercourse breaks the oath; under the Shafi'i definition, only the contract does.
This affects rulings on oaths, divorces, and other legal assumptions.
4
Recognize the Hanafi tarjih
The author declares the Hanafi view as 'al-sahih' (the correct one). This is the relied-upon position in the school.
The text does not elaborate on proofs, but later Hanafi works provide detailed arguments.

Source Text

بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
حامداً ومصليّاً ومسلماً، يقول الراجي عفو ربِّه القوي أبو الحسنات محمد عبد الحي اللكْنَوِي - تجاوز الله عن ذنبه الجلي والخفي - : هذا هو الربع الثاني من «عمدة الرعاية في حل شرح الوقاية» المتعلقة بربع الثاني من شرح الوقاية أرجو من الله الذي وفقني للشروع فيه أن يوفقني لاختتامه ولإتمام الربعين الأخيرين بفضله وإحسانه.
١ ] قوله : كتاب النكاح ؛ أي هذا كتاب في بيان أحكام النكاح ، – وهو بالكسر – : لغة: الضم والجمع ، ومن أفراده وطء الزوجة، وكذا قيل : إنه حقيقة في الوطء لغة، واختلفوا في معناه الحقيقي شرعاً ، فنُسب إلى الشافعي له أنه شرعاً حقيقة في العقد، مجاز في الوطء، والصحيح عند أصحابنا أنه حقيقة شرعاً أيضاً في الوطء، مجاز في النكاح. وقيل : إنه مشترك لفظي بينهما، وهو ضعيف. وقيل : مشترك معنوي. وإنما ذكر المصنف له أحكام النكاح فيما بين أحكام المعاملات المحضة والعبادات المحضة ؛ لكونه معاملة من وجه وعبادة من وجه.
الزنا ()
١ . وهو واجب عند شدة الاشتياق والشهوة بحيث يغلب على ظنه وقوعه في الزنا ()
(۱) وهذا القسم مشروط بشرطين : ١ . أن يكون مالكاً للمهر والنفقة ، فليس من خافه إذا كان عاجزاً عنهما آثم بتركه. قيد بهذا الشرط : الكاساني في «البدائع» (۲ : ۲۲۹)، وتبعه ابن الهمام في «فتح القدير»(۳ : ۲۸۷)، وابن نجیم في «البحر» (٢ : ٨٤) ، والشرنبلالي في حاشية الدرر (١) : (٣٢٦)، والحصكفي في «الــدر المختار» (۲) : (٢٦٠) ، وقيده ابن عابدين في رد المحتار (۲) : ٢٦٠) بلو كان بالاستدانة. ۲. عدم خوف الجور ؛ فإن تعارض خوف الوقوع في الزنا لو لم يتزوج وخوف الجور لو تزوج قدم خوف الجور، فلا افتراض حينئذ، بل مكروه؛ لأن الجور معصية متعلقة بالعباد، والمنع من الزنا من حقوق الله ، وحق العبد مقدّم عند التعارض لاحتياجه وغنى المولى . قيد بهذا
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praising and sending blessings and peace, he who hopes for the pardon of his Lord, the Strong, Abu al-Hasanat Muhammad Abd al-Hayy al-Laknawi - may Allah forgive his evident and hidden sin - says: This is the second quarter of 'Umda al-Ri'aya fi Hall Sharh al-Wiqaya' related to the second quarter of Sharh al-Wiqaya. I hope from Allah, who has granted me success to begin it, that He grants me success to conclude it and to complete the remaining two quarters by His grace and beneficence. 1] His statement: The Book of Marriage; that is, this is a book explaining the rulings of marriage. It (with a kasra) linguistically means: joining and gathering, and among its particular meanings is intercourse with the wife, and likewise it is said: it is a reality in intercourse linguistically. They disagreed regarding its true meaning legally. It is attributed to al-Shafi'i that legally it is a reality in the contract, a metaphor in intercourse. The correct view according to our scholars is that it is a reality legally as well in intercourse, a metaphor in marriage. And it is said: it is a verbal homonym between the two, and this is weak. And it is said: it is a common meaning. The author only mentioned the rulings of marriage between the rulings of pure transactions and pure acts of worship because it is a transaction in one respect and worship in another. Zina () 1. It (marriage) is obligatory at the time of intense desire and lust such that it is strongly probable that he would fall into zina (fornication) (1) This category is conditioned by two conditions: 1. That he possess the dowry and maintenance, so one who fears falling into sin if he is incapable of these is not sinful by leaving it. This condition was stipulated by al-Kasani in al-Bada'i' (2:229), followed by Ibn al-Humann in Fath al-Qadir (3:287), Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr (2:84), al-Shurunbulali in the gloss on al-Durar (1:326), al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:260), and it was conditioned by Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2:260) even if by borrowing. 2. Absence of fear of injustice; if there is a conflict between fear of falling into zina if he does not marry and fear of injustice if he marries, the fear of injustice is given precedence, so then it is not obligatory, rather it is disliked; because injustice is a sin relating to the servants, while refraining from zina is from the rights of Allah, and the right of the servant is given precedence when they conflict due to his need and the Lord's self-sufficiency. This condition was stipulated by...
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is the opening commentary on the Book of Marriage from Umda al-Ri'aya. The author begins by invoking the name of Allah and identifying this as the second quarter of his work. He then comments on the matn (base text) stating 'Kitab al-Nikah' (The Book of Marriage). The commentary explains the linguistic meaning of nikah: it denotes joining and gathering, with one particular application being intercourse with one's wife. The legal meaning is contested: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that nikah is a legal reality for the contract (al-aqd) and a metaphor for intercourse, while the Hanafi school holds the opposite—that it is a legal reality for intercourse and a metaphor for the contract. The author notes other opinions (verbal homonym, common meaning) but considers them weak. He then explains why the author of the base text placed marriage between pure transactions and pure acts of worship: because marriage partakes of both aspects. The passage then turns to the ruling of marriage. It states that marriage is wajib (obligatory) when one fears falling into zina due to overwhelming desire. A footnote (likely from the commentator or editor) provides two conditions for this obligation: (1) one must be able to afford the dower and maintenance, even by borrowing according to some; (2) one must not fear committing injustice (zulm) by marrying, such as abusing one's spouse. If there is a conflict, preventing injustice takes precedence, so marriage becomes makruh instead of wajib. This hierarchy reflects the Hanafi principle that the rights of fellow humans (huquq al-ibad) take precedence over the rights of Allah (huquq Allah) when they conflict.

Key Terms
النكاح (al-nikah): Linguistically: joining and gathering; legally in the Hanafi school: a reality in sexual intercourse and a metaphor for the marriage contract.
الواطء (al-wat'): Sexual intercourse; one of the specific meanings of nikah.
العقد (al-aqd): The marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage, what is the legal reality (haqiqah shar'iyyah) of nikah?
A) The marriage contract (al-aqd)
B) Sexual intercourse (al-wat')
C) Both equally
D) The dower (al-mahr)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines nikah as a legal reality in intercourse, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i school's contract-based definition, with practical effects on oaths and legal rulings.

Nikah — Marriage

7 min
When Is Marriage Obligatory? Conditions for Wujub
Opening commentary on the Book of Marriage explaining the obligatory nature of marriage under specific conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which marriage becomes wajib in the Hanafi school and apply the rule of preference between huquq Allah and huquq al-ibad when they conflict.

Core Idea
Marriage is only wajib when you fear zina, can afford it, and won't commit injustice — otherwise it is not obligatory.

While marriage is generally recommended, the Hanafi school specifies when it becomes compulsory. This lesson examines the two conditions mentioned in the commentary: affordability and absence of fear of injustice, and the legal reasoning behind preferring the right of the servant over Allah's right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling and the exception
Note that the passage states marriage is wajib 'at the time of intense desire...' This indicates a conditional obligation, not an absolute one.
The default ruling for marriage is istihbab (recommended).
2
Recognize the two conditions for wujub
The footnote enumerates: (1) possession of mahr and nafaqah, (2) absence of fear of injustice. Both must be met.
If either condition fails, marriage is not wajib.
3
Understand the conflict resolution
When fear of zina and fear of injustice coexist, the passage gives precedence to preventing injustice. This is because injustice harms a fellow human (haqq al-'abd), while zina is a right of Allah (haqq Allah).
The principle: haqq al-'abd is preferred due to the servant's need and Allah's self-sufficiency.
4
Note the authority and reliability
The passage cites several authoritative Hanafi works (al-Bada'i', Fath al-Qadir, etc.) that stipulate these conditions, indicating the mufta bihi position.
This is from the fatawa mutakhkhirin (later fatwa literature).
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a case: a man fears zina but also fears he may harm his wife financially or physically. Since fear of injustice takes precedence, he should not marry, and it is not sinful to refrain.
If he marries despite the fear, the ruling is makruh tahrimi (close to haram)? The passage says 'makruh' (disliked) without specifying the degree, but later Hanafis clarify it is makruh tahrimi.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is the opening commentary on the Book of Marriage from Umda al-Ri'aya. The author begins by invoking the name of Allah and identifying this as the second quarter of his work. He then comments on the matn (base text) stating 'Kitab al-Nikah' (The Book of Marriage). He then explains why the author of the base text placed marriage between pure transactions and pure acts of worship: because marriage partakes of both aspects. The passage then turns to the ruling of marriage. It states that marriage is wajib (obligatory) when one fears falling into zina due to overwhelming desire. النكاح (al-nikah): Linguistically: joining and gathering; legally in the Hanafi school: a reality in sexual intercourse and a metaphor for the marriage contract.
الواطء (al-wat'): Sexual intercourse; one of the specific meanings of nikah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what takes precedence when there is conflict between fear of falling into zina and fear of committing injustice in marriage?
A) Fear of zina because it is a major sin
B) Fear of injustice because rights of servants precede rights of Allah
C) Both are equal
D) Neither, marriage becomes haram

Key Takeaway
Marriage is wajib only when one fears zina, can afford it, and does not fear injustice; otherwise, it is not obligatory, and preventing injustice takes priority over avoiding zina.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
The Hanafi Classification of Marriage: Definition and Five Rulings
Definition and five rulings of marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of nikah, identify the five possible rulings of marriage, and explain the conditions under which each ruling applies.

Core Idea
Marriage has five possible rulings under Hanafi law: obligatory, recommended, disliked, forbidden, or permissible depending on circumstance.

Marriage (nikah) is a central institution in Islamic law, bridging acts of worship and social transactions. This lesson introduces you to the opening of Kitab al-Nikah in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, where the author defines nikah linguistically and legally, and then enumerates the five rulings that marriage can take based on the individual's situation. You will learn how the Hanafi school distinguishes the legal meaning of nikah from that of al-Shafi'i, and how classical evidences support the flexible classification of marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What legal act is being discussed? Marriage (nikah). What are its possible rulings?
2
Understand the definition dispute
How do the Hanafis and Shafi'is define nikah legally? What is the practical consequence?
3
List the five cases
Under what conditions is marriage wajib, sunnah muakkada, makruh, haram, or mubah?
4
Examine the evidence for sunnah status
How does the hadith of the Prophet support the non-obligatory nature?
5
Note the restrictions on obligation
What two conditions must be present for marriage to become wajib? What if injustice is feared?

حامداً ومصليّاً ومسلماً، يقول الراجي عفو ربِّه القوي أبو الحسنات محمد عبد الحي اللكْنَوِي - تجاوز الله عن ذنبه الجلي والخفي - : هذا هو الربع الثاني من «عمدة الرعاية في حل شرح الوقاية» المتعلقة بربع الثاني من شرح الوقاية أرجو من الله الذي وفقني للشروع فيه أن يوفقني لاختتامه ولإتمام الربعين الأخيرين بفضله وإحسانه. ١ ] قوله : كتاب النكاح ؛ أي هذا كتاب في بيان أحكام النكاح ، – وهو بالكسر – : لغة: الضم والجمع ، ومن أفراده وطء الزوجة، وكذا قيل : إنه حقيقة في الوطء لغة، واختلفوا في معناه الحقيقي شرعاً ، فنُسب إلى الشافعي له أنه شرعاً حقيقة في العقد، مجاز في الوطء، والصحيح عند أصحابنا أنه حقيقة شرعاً أيضاً في الوطء، مجاز في النكاح. وقيل : إنه مشترك لفظي بينهما، وهو ضعيف.

الكاساني في «البدائع (۲) : (۲۲۹)، وتبعه ابن الهمام في فتح القدير»(۳ : ۲۸۷)، وابن نجیم في «البحر» (٢ : ٨٤) ، والشرنبلالي في حاشية الدرر (١) : (٣٢٦)، والحصكفي في «الــدر المختار» (۲) : (٢٦٠) ، وقيده ابن عابدين في رد المحتار (۲) : ٢٦٠) بلو كان بالاستدانة. ۲. عدم خوف الجور ؛ فإن تعارض خوف الوقوع في الزنا لو لم يتزوج وخوف الجور لو تزوج قدم خوف الجور، فلا افتراض حينئذ، بل مكروه؛ لأن الجور معصية متعلقة بالعباد، والمنع من الزنا من حقوق الله ، وحق العبد مقدّم عند التعارض لاحتياجه وغنى المولى . قيد بهذا
الشرط ابن الهمام في «الفتح» (۳) : (۱۸۷) ، وتبعه ابن نجيم في البحر »(۳: ۸۳)، وابن عابدین في رد المحتار» (٢ : ٢٦١). صرح به صاحب «المحيط» و «الفتح» و «المختار» (۳ : ۱۰۸)، و«الملتقى»(ص٤٩)، و«البحر» (٢: ٨٤)، و(الدر المختار» و رد المحتار (۲) : (٢٦١)، وغيرهم، ومن الأدلة على السنية في حالة الاعتدال :
ا قوله : (يا معشر الشباب من استطاع منكم الباءة فليتزوج فإنه أغض للبصر وأحصن للفرج ومن لم يستطع فعليه بالصوم فإنه له وجاء في (صحيح مسلم (۲ : ۱۰۱۸)، و«صحيح البخاري (۲) : (٦٧٣) ؛ إذ أقام الصوم مقام النكاح ، والصوم ليس بواجب فدل على أن النكاح ليس بواجب أيضا ، لأن غير الواجب لا يقوم مقام الواجب. قوله الله النفر الثلاثة: «لكني أصلي وأنام وأصوم وأفطر وأتزوج النساء فمن رغب عن سنتي فليس مني» في صحيح مسلم (۲) : (۱۰۲۰) ، و(صحيح البخاري»(٥: ١٩٤٩)، وغيرها. .قوله: «من أحب فطرتي فليستن بسنتي، ومن سنتي النكاح في «مصنف عبد الرزاق»(٦ : ١٦٩)، و (سنن البيهقي الكبير (۷) : (۷۷) ، و(مسند أبي يعلى(٥: ١٣٣)، وشعب الإيمان (٤) : (۳۸۱) ، قال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (٤) : ٢٥٢) : رجاله ثقات ..... (۲) وهذا عند تمكنه من الاحتراز عنه كعدم كفايتها حاجتها من الوطء ، والكراهة هنا تحريمية كما صرح به ابن نجيم في البحر الرائق»(٢ : ٨٤). (۳) أي تيقّنَ الرجلُ عدم القيام بأمور الزوجية من كفاية زوجته حاجتها من الجماع ؛ لأن الزواج شرع لكفاية كل منها الآخر رغبته ، وبعدم قدرته على ذلك يكون الجور عليها ؛ وتعريضها للانحراف، وهو مشروع لمصلحة تحصين النفس وتحصيل الثواب، وبالجور يأثم ويرتكب المحرمات، فتنعدم المصالح لرجحان هذه المفاسد . ينظر : «البحر الرائق»(٢ : ٨٤).
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praising and sending blessings and peace, the one hoping for the forgiveness of his Mighty Lord, Abu al-Hasanat Muhammad Abd al-Hayy al-Laknawi - may Allah forgive his manifest and hidden sin - says: This is the second quarter of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah fi Hall Sharh al-Wiqayah related to the second quarter of Sharh al-Wiqayah. I hope from Allah, who guided me to begin it, to guide me to complete it and to complete the last two quarters by His grace and beneficence. 1] His saying: Kitab al-Nikah; i.e., this is a book on explaining the rulings of marriage. And it (with kasrah) linguistically means: joining and gathering. And among its individual meanings is sexual intercourse with the wife. Likewise, it is said that it is literally used for intercourse linguistically. And they differed regarding its true meaning legally. It is attributed to al-Shafi'i that it is legally literally used for the contract and metaphorically for intercourse. The correct view according to our scholars is that it is also legally literally used for intercourse and metaphorically for marriage. And it is said that it is a homonym between them, which is weak. And it is said it is a common term. The author only mentioned the rulings of marriage between the rulings of pure transactions and pure acts of worship because it is a transaction from one aspect and worship from another. Adultery () 1. It (marriage) is obligatory when desire and passion are intense such that he is convinced he will fall into it (namely, adultery). (1) This category is conditional upon two conditions: 1. He possesses the dowry and maintenance; so one who fears but is unable to afford them is not sinful for leaving it. This condition was stipulated by al-Kasani in al-Bada'i' (2:229), followed by Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir (3:287), Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr (2:84), al-Shurunbulali in Hashiyat al-Durar (1:326), al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:260), and Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2:260) even if by borrowing. 2. No fear of injustice; if fear of falling into zina if he does not marry conflicts with fear of injustice if he marries, then fear of injustice takes precedence, so it is not obligatory then, rather disliked; because injustice is a sin concerning the rights of people, while preventing zina is from the rights of Allah, and the right of the servant is given precedence in the case of conflict due to his need and the Lord's self-sufficiency. This condition was stipulated by Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath (3:187), followed by Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr (3:83), and Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2:261). It was stated clearly by the author of al-Muhit, al-Fath, al-Mukhtar (3:108), al-Multaqa (p.49), al-Bahr (2:84), al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and Radd al-Muhtar (2:261), and others. And from the evidences for it being sunnah in a state of moderation: his saying: 'O young men, whoever among you can afford marriage, let him marry, for it is more lowering of the gaze and more guarding of the private parts; and whoever cannot, then let him fast, for it is a protection for him' in Sahih Muslim (2:1018) and Sahih al-Bukhari (2:673). For he made fasting stand in place of marriage, and fasting is not obligatory, so it indicates that marriage is also not obligatory, because a non-obligatory act does not stand in place of an obligatory one. His saying to the three men: 'But I pray and sleep, fast and break fast, and marry women; whoever turns away from my way is not from me' in Sahih Muslim (2:1020) and Sahih al-Bukhari (5:1949) and others. His saying: 'Whoever loves my natural disposition should follow my way, and from my way is marriage' in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq (6:169), Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (7:77), Musnad Abi Ya'la (5:133), and Shu'ab al-Iman (4:381). Al-Haythami said in Majma' al-Zawa'id (4:252): Its narrators are trustworthy... (2) This (disliked) is when he can avoid it, like not fulfilling her need of intercourse. And the disliked here is prohibitively disliked, as stated by Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:84). (3) That is, the man is certain of his inability to fulfill marital duties, such as satisfying his wife's need for intercourse; because marriage was legislated for each to fulfill the other's desire, and by inability he would be unjust to her and expose her to deviation, while it is legislated for the benefit of protecting oneself and earning reward; with injustice he sins and commits forbidden acts, so the benefits are nullified due to the preponderance of these harms. See: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:84).
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Marriage (Kitab al-Nikah) from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah. The author first defines nikah linguistically as 'joining' and 'gathering,' and clarifies that in legal terminology, the Hanafi school holds that nikah is literally used for the act of sexual intercourse, while metaphorically for the marriage contract. This contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that nikah legally means the contract, not intercourse. The placement of the marriage chapter between transactions and worship is explained because marriage partakes of both: social dealings (mu'amalat) from the perspective of dower, maintenance, etc., and worship from the perspective of obedience, chastity, and fulfilling religious duties.

Then the five rulings of marriage are enumerated: (1) wajib (obligatory) when one strongly fears falling into zina (illicit sexual relations) due to overwhelming desire, provided one can afford the mahr and nafaqa and does not fear injustice in marriage. (2) Sunnah mu'akkada (emphasized sunna) in a state of moderate desire, supported by hadiths encouraging marriage. (3) Makruh (disliked) when one fears being unjust to one's wife, e.g., inability to fulfill her sexual needs. (4) Haram (forbidden) when certain of inability to fulfill marital obligations, as it would cause harm. (5) Mubah (permissible) when uncertain about ability or simply desiring companionship without strong fear of zina.

Conditions for obligation are detailed: the person must have the means and must not anticipate injustice. If fear of zina conflicts with fear of injustice, the latter takes precedence because rights of fellow humans are prioritized over divine rights in such conflicts. Evidences for the sunna ruling include the famous hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: 'O young men, whoever can afford marriage, let him marry...' and the Prophet's statement that marriage is his way.

Key Terms
النكاح (al-nikah): Linguistically: joining/gathering; legally: according to Hanafis, literally for sexual intercourse, metaphorically for the marriage contract; according to Shafi'is, literally for the contract.
الباءة (al-bā'a): The ability to consummate marriage, interpreted as financial capability for mahr and maintenance, or sexual potency.
السنة المؤكدة (al-sunnah al-mu'akkadah): An emphasized sunnah, which is recommended but not obligatory; omitting it is disliked but not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is marriage (nikah) considered obligatory (wajib) according to this passage?
A) When one has strong sexual desire and can afford it, even if he fears injustice.
B) When one fears falling into zina due to intense desire, possesses the means (mahr and nafaqa), and does not fear injustice in marriage.
C) When one wants to have children and can support a family.
D) When one's parents command it.

Key Takeaway
Marriage in Hanafi law is a flexible act whose ruling varies from wajib to haram based on an individual's capacity and circumstances.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
7 min
The Hanafi Definition of Nikah
Definition of nikah as a contract for milk al-mut'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of nikah and distinguish its key components: contract (aqd), ownership (milk), and enjoyment (mut'ah).

Core Idea
Nikah is a contract for ownership of enjoyment — legal permissibility, not proprietary ownership.

Nikah (marriage) is a fundamental contract in Islamic law. The Hanafi school defines it with precision: 'a contract for ownership of enjoyment' (aql mawdu' li milk al-mut'ah). This lesson unpacks each term to understand what the contract grants and what it does not. The commentator stresses that 'ownership' here means permissibility (hill), not actual property rights, as proven by the rule that if the wife is subjected to shubhah intercourse, her dower goes to her, not the husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'nikah' in its legal sense. Note that the author clarifies that here nikah means the contract (aqd), not the act of intercourse (wat').
This is crucial because the rulings of nikah pertain to the contract.
2
Extract the core definition
The definition is: 'It is a contract established for ownership of enjoyment.' Write down the Arabic: عقد موضوع لملك المتعة.
Each word is deliberately chosen.
3
Analyze each component
Break down the definition: (1) 'aqd' – contract, not intercourse. (2) 'milk' – here means legal permissibility (hill), not proprietary ownership. (3) 'mut'ah' – enjoyment, including all lawful sexual benefit.
The commentator explains that 'milk' is like the lam in 'letters are placed for combination' – indicating purpose.
4
Understand the proof
The passage provides a key proof: if the husband truly owned the benefit, then in shubhah intercourse the dower (mahr) would go to him, but it goes to the wife. This shows the husband only has permissibility (hill), not ownership.
This proof is a critical argument in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Note the scope and differences
The definition applies to the husband's right, but the wife also has similar rights. Some Hanafi scholars differ on whether it is 'ownership of the person' or 'ownership of benefits'.
These differences appear within the school.

Book of Nikah / its definition, ruling, pillar, and conditions: It is a contract established for ownership of enjoyment: that is, permissibility of enjoyment by the man. (1) (2) And the detail of these divisions with the differences is mentioned in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [1] His saying: 'It is a contract' indicates that by nikah here is meant the contract, not intercourse, for the author is concerned with explaining its rulings. [2] His saying: 'for ownership' – the lam here is like the lam in the saying 'the letters of the alphabet are placed for combination' i.e., it is their purpose and goal, not like the lam entering upon the object. [3] His saying: 'al-mut'ah' – with damm, it is a noun for something that is benefited from, and it also comes in the meaning of enjoyment. Both are possible here. He said in al-Bada'i': 'From its rulings is ownership of enjoyment, which is the husband's exclusive right to the benefits of her private parts and all her limbs for enjoyment, or ownership of the self and person with respect to enjoyment, according to the difference among our shaykhs.' [4] His saying: 'hill' – with kasr of the important ha and tashdid of the lam. [0] His saying: 'enjoyment' – that is, seeking benefit and making use in any manner not prohibited by the Shari'ah, like enjoyment through sodomy, for the hadith came: 'Cursed is the one who comes to a woman in her anus' – narrated by the authors of the Sunan. [6] His saying: 'the man' – it may be objected that this specification is harmful, for just as marriage benefits the man... (1) From the matns like al-Tanwir (p.55), al-Multaqa (p.49), al-Mukhtar (3:109), al-Ghurar (1:326), that the division is threefold: wujub, sunniyyah, and karahah. And this sixfold division with the addition of fard was also mentioned by Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir (3:187), Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:84), al-Shurunbulali in al-Shurunbulaliyyah (1:326), and Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2:261). Among them, some made it fivefold and did not mention mubah, like al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:260-261) and others. (2) al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:84). (3) Also, Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr (3:85) interpreted 'milk al-mut'ah' as permissibility of enjoyment, and Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2:258-259) objected to him: that interpretation as 'exclusive right' is better, because exclusive right is closer to the meaning of ownership, as ownership is a type of it, unlike permissibility, because it is a necessary consequence of ownership of enjoyment, and it is necessary for her exclusive right to the husband in the Shari'ah. And the intended meaning of 'milk' is permissibility (hill), not legal ownership, because if the wife is copulated with under shubhah, her dower goes to her; if he actually owned the benefit of her private parts, its substitute would be due to him.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of nikah (marriage) from the Hanafi perspective, along with a detailed commentary. The main definition states that nikah is 'a contract established for ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah)'. The commentator then explains each term: 'contract' (aqd) to distinguish it from the act of intercourse (wat'); 'ownership' (milk) here means legal permissibility (hill), not actual ownership, as proven by the rule that if there is shubhah intercourse, the dower goes to the wife, not the husband (the substitute for the benefit would go to the owner if he truly owned it). 'Enjoyment' (mut'ah) includes all forms of lawful sexual enjoyment. The passage also clarifies the difference between interpreting 'milk al-mut'ah' as permissibility (hill) versus exclusive right (ikhtisas). Ibn Nujaym favored the former, but Ibn Abidin corrected that 'exclusive right' is more accurate because it aligns with the concept of ownership. The text also includes a discussion on the different divisions of the rulings of nikah (threefold, fivefold, sixfold) attributed to various Hanafi authorities.

Key Terms
نكاح (nikah): Legally, the marriage contract, not the act of intercourse, as the author is discussing its rulings.
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut'ah): Ownership of enjoyment; the husband's right to enjoy the wife's person (e.g., private parts and other limbs) lawfully.
حل (hill): Legal permissibility; the license to engage in sexual relations, as opposed to actual ownership (milk haqiqi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, what does 'milk' in 'milk al-mut'ah' primarily mean?
A) Actual ownership of the wife's body
B) Legal permissibility (hill) of enjoyment
C) Exclusive ownership of her property
D) A metaphor for love and affection

Key Takeaway
Nikah is a contract granting legal permissibility of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah), not ownership of the spouse's person or property.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Refining Milk al-Mut'ah: Permissibility vs. Exclusive Right
Critical refinement of the definition of milk al-mut'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Abidin preferred interpreting milk al-mut'ah as ikhtisas (exclusive right) rather than hill (permissibility), and evaluate the practical impact.

Core Idea
Milk al-mut'ah is an exclusive right (ikhtisas), not mere permissibility — permissibility is its consequence.

In the earlier lesson, we learned that milk al-mut'ah means permissibility (hill). However, the commentator Ibn Abidin, in his work Radd al-Muhtar, refined this view. He argued that interpreting it as 'exclusive right' (ikhtisas) is more precise because it aligns with the concept of ownership and better explains the legal effects. This lesson delves into that refinement, which is an intra-Hanafi scholarly difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq interprets milk al-mut'ah as hill (permissibility). Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar corrects this, saying it is ikhtisas (exclusive right).
Both are Hanafi scholars; this is a refinement within the school.
2
Understand Ibn Abidin's reasoning
Ibn Abidin argues: exclusive right is closer to the meaning of ownership (milk) because ownership is a type of exclusive right. Permissibility (hill) is a necessary consequence of that exclusive right, not the right itself.
The relationship is: exclusive right → permissibility. The latter flows from the former.
3
Analyze the shubhah proof in light of both views
The shubhah proof shows that the husband does not have actual ownership. Both views agree on this. But Ibn Abidin's view explains why the dower goes to the wife: because the husband only has a right to permissibility, which is ineffective against the wife's ownership. Under the exclusive right view, the husband's right is a specific claim over the benefits, but not ownership of the person.
The proof does not force a choice between hill and ikhtisas; it merely rules out actual ownership.
4
Recognize the practical difference
The difference is largely conceptual, but it affects how we understand the rights in marriage. For example, if we say 'exclusive right', it emphasizes that the husband has a claim that excludes others; if we say 'permissibility', it emphasizes that the husband may enjoy but the wife retains ownership. In practice, both lead to the same legal rules, but the conceptual framing influences fatwa.
The mufta bihi is that milk al-mut'ah means hill, but the ikhtisas view is accepted for precision.

Book of Nikah / its definition, ruling, pillar, and conditions: It is a contract established for ownership of enjoyment: that is, permissibility of enjoyment by the man. (1) (2) And the detail of these divisions with the differences is mentioned in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [1] His saying: 'It is a contract' indicates that by nikah here is meant the contract, not intercourse, for the author is concerned with explaining its rulings. [2] His saying: 'for ownership' – the lam here is like the lam in the saying 'the letters of the alphabet are placed for combination' i.e., it is their purpose and goal, not like the lam entering upon the object. [3] His saying: 'al-mut'ah' – with damm, it is a noun for something that is benefited from, and it also comes in the meaning of enjoyment. Both are possible here. He said in al-Bada'i': 'From its rulings is ownership of enjoyment, which is the husband's exclusive right to the benefits of her private parts and all her limbs for enjoyment, or ownership of the self and person with respect to enjoyment, according to the difference among our shaykhs.' [4] His saying: 'hill' – with kasr of the important ha and tashdid of the lam. [0] His saying: 'enjoyment' – that is, seeking benefit and making use in any manner not prohibited by the Shari'ah, like enjoyment through sodomy, for the hadith came: 'Cursed is the one who comes to a woman in her anus' – narrated by the authors of the Sunan. [6] His saying: 'the man' – it may be objected that this specification is harmful, for just as marriage benefits the man... (1) From the matns like al-Tanwir (p.55), al-Multaqa (p.49), al-Mukhtar (3:109), al-Ghurar (1:326), that the division is threefold: wujub, sunniyyah, and karahah. And this sixfold division with the addition of fard was also mentioned by Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir (3:187), Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:84), al-Shurunbulali in al-Shurunbulaliyyah (1:326), and Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2:261). Among them, some made it fivefold and did not mention mubah, like al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:260-261) and others. (2) al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:84). (3) Also, Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr (3:85) interpreted 'milk al-mut'ah' as permissibility of enjoyment, and Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2:258-259) objected to him: that interpretation as 'exclusive right' is better, because exclusive right is closer to the meaning of ownership, as ownership is a type of it, unlike permissibility, because it is a necessary consequence of ownership of enjoyment, and it is necessary for her exclusive right to the husband in the Shari'ah. And the intended meaning of 'milk' is permissibility (hill), not legal ownership, because if the wife is copulated with under shubhah, her dower goes to her; if he actually owned the benefit of her private parts, its substitute would be due to him.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of nikah (marriage) from the Hanafi perspective, along with a detailed commentary. The main definition states that nikah is 'a contract established for ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah)'. The commentator then explains each term: 'contract' (aqd) to distinguish it from the act of intercourse (wat'); 'ownership' (milk) here means legal permissibility (hill), not actual ownership, as proven by the rule that if there is shubhah intercourse, the dower goes to the wife, not the husband (the substitute for the benefit would go to the owner if he truly owned it). 'Enjoyment' (mut'ah) includes all forms of lawful sexual enjoyment. The passage also clarifies the difference between interpreting 'milk al-mut'ah' as permissibility (hill) versus exclusive right (ikhtisas). Ibn Nujaym favored the former, but Ibn Abidin corrected that 'exclusive right' is more accurate because it aligns with the concept of ownership. The text also includes a discussion on the different divisions of the rulings of nikah (threefold, fivefold, sixfold) attributed to various Hanafi authorities.

Key Terms
اختصاص (ikhtisas): Exclusive right; the husband's unique claim to enjoy the wife's person, to the exclusion of others.
حل (hill): Legal permissibility; the husband's license to enjoy the wife lawfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Abidin prefer 'exclusive right (ikhtisas)' over 'permissibility (hill)'?
A) Because the Prophet used the term 'exclusive right'.
B) Because exclusive right is closer to the meaning of ownership, and permissibility is a consequence.
C) Because permissibility contradicts the shubhah proof.
D) Because exclusive right implies the wife has no rights.

Key Takeaway
Milk al-mut'ah is best understood as an exclusive right (ikhtisas) that grants permissibility (hill); both interpretations are valid, but the relied-upon view is hill.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
The Hanafi Definition of Nikah: A Contract for Ownership of Enjoyment
Defining nikah as a contract for lawful enjoyment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of nikah, explain each component (contract, ownership, enjoyment), and apply it to distinguish marriage from other relationships.

Core Idea
Nikah in Hanafi fiqh is a civil contract granting the husband lawful ownership of enjoyment from his wife.

In this passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, the author defines 'nikah' as it is used in legal discourse. Unlike common usage, where nikah can refer to the marriage celebration or the act of intercourse, the Hanafi legal tradition pinpoints it as a specific contract (عقد) with the purpose of establishing ownership of enjoyment (ملك المتعة). This definition shapes many subsequent rulings, from mahr to maintenance. Understanding this precise definition is the first step to grasping the Hanafi law of marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term and its scope
The passage clarifies that nikah here means the contract, not intercourse. Note the author's explicit statement: 'indicates that what is intended by nikah here is the contract'.
2
Break down the definition's components
The definition is 'a contract made for ownership of enjoyment'. Identify each component: contract (عقد), ownership (ملك), enjoyment (متعة), and the implied permissibility (حل). Understand how the commentary expands each.
The lam in 'li-milk' indicates purpose, like 'letters made for combination'.
3
Analyze the objection about gender
The passage raises an objection: why specify 'the man' when the contract benefits both? The response: the man is mentioned because he is the owner of the right to enforce enjoyment, though both have the right. This teaches that legal definitions may highlight the primary right-holder.
This objection-response pattern is common in Hanafi legal texts.
4
Understand 'ownership of enjoyment' (milk al-mut'ah)
This is a unique Hanafi concept. It means the husband's exclusive right to derive lawful pleasure from his wife's body. The passage notes an intra-Hanafi difference: is it ownership of the benefit (manafi') or of the self (dhat) for enjoyment? The relied-upon view is benefit ownership.
This distinction affects rulings on mahr and compensation for injuries.
5
Connect to the contract's pillars: offer and acceptance
The commentary defines 'contract' as the binding of parts of a legal transaction (ījāb and qabūl). In nikah, the offer and acceptance are the pillars. The passage illustrates with examples: 'zawwajtu' and 'tazawwaju' create the legal meaning of marriage.
The performative nature of these utterances is key; they bring the ruling into existence.

Book of Marriage / Its definition, ruling, pillar, and conditions.
It is:
[2]
[0]
(A contract) made for ownership of enjoyment: meaning permissibility (4) of enjoyment (5) of the man (6)
(1) (2) And the detail of these categories together with the differences is mentioned in 'Al-Bahr al-Raiq' [1] His statement: 'It is a contract' indicates that what is intended by nikah here is the contract, not sexual intercourse, for the author is in the process of explaining its rulings, not its rulings. [2] His statement: 'for ownership' – the lam here is like the lam in their statement: 'The alphabetical letters are made for combination': i.e., it is its end and purpose, not like the lam entering upon the subject matter. [3] His statement: 'al-mut'ah' – it is with dammah, a noun for that which is benefited from, and it also comes in the meaning of enjoyment, and all are possible here. He said in 'Al-Bada'i': 'Indeed, from its rulings is ownership of enjoyment, and it is the husband's exclusive right to the benefits of her private part and all her limbs for enjoyment, or for ownership of the self and person in terms of enjoyment, according to the difference of our mashāyikh in that.' [4] His statement: 'permissibility' – with kasr of the ḥā' and tashdīd of the lām. [0] His statement: 'enjoyment' – meaning seeking benefit and usage in any manner as long as the Law does not prohibit it, like enjoyment through sodomy, for it has come in the hadith: 'Cursed is he who approaches a woman in her anus,' narrated by the compilers of Sunan. [6] His statement: 'the man' – an objection may be raised against this specification that it is harmful, for nikah just as it gives permissibility of enjoyment of the man from the woman, similarly it gives permissibility of enjoyment of the woman from the man as well. And its answer: The mention of the man is either because he is nobler than the woman, or because he is the possessor of the right without her, even though permissibility of enjoyment is from both sides, for he has the right to compel her to intercourse if she refuses without a legal impediment, while she does not have the right to compel him after he has had intercourse once, even though it is incumbent upon him to do so sometimes as a matter of religious duty. (1) His statement: 'from the woman' – i.e., from the one whom no legal impediment prevents from marriage; thus excluded is male with male, the hermaphrodite absolutely, the jinnī with a human, and those women who are permanently forbidden (to marry). Thus in 'Al-‘Ināyah'. (2) (2) His statement: 'So the contract' – an explanation of the definition of the contract and what is intended by it in the definition of marriage. (3) His statement: 'i.e., offer and acceptance' – The offer (ījāb): it is affirmation linguistically, and by it is called the first speech of one of the two contracting parties; and the speech of the other that is based on it is called acceptance (qabūl) with dammah. [4] His statement: 'But here...' etc. – He intends by it that the contract, even though originally meaning binding, (1) What is intended by 'the contract resulting from the verbal noun' is the connection of parts of the legal transaction, i.e., the connected parts like 'zawwajtu' (I married) and 'tazawwaju' (I married), and similarly 'bi'tu' (I sold) and 'ishtaraytu' (I bought); for the Lawgiver has made some declarative compounds performative such that when they occur, a legal meaning occurs along with them, upon which a legal ruling follows. For example, when it is said: 'zawwajtu' and 'tazawwaju', a legal meaning occurs, which is marriage, and a legal ruling follows from it, which is ownership of enjoyment. Similarly, when it is said: 'bi'tu' and 'ishtaraytu', a legal meaning occurs, which is sale, and a legal ruling follows from it, which is ownership of possession. And since between the performative expression and its meaning there is a strong relationship such that the meaning does not lag behind it—because speech act is the bringing into existence of a meaning by an expression that accompanies it in existence—the performative expressions are named by the names of their meanings, as they mentioned 'sale' and 'marriage' and intended by them the offer and acceptance; and thus marriage is used here for the contract, even though the contract is placed for marriage legally. Its completion is in 'Durar al-Ḥukkām' (1:327). (2) Up to here is mentioned in 'Al-‘Ināyah' (3:187). (3) This addition is mentioned in 'Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq' (3:83), appended to the speech of the author of 'Al-‘Ināyah' after its transmission, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This is a definition-ruling passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqāyah, where the author defines the term 'nikah' (marriage) as used in the context of legal rulings. The passage clarifies that in this context, nikah refers to the contract of marriage, not the physical act of intercourse. The definition is 'a contract made for ownership of enjoyment' (عقد موضوع لملك المتعة), meaning it is a legal agreement that grants the husband the lawful right to seek pleasure from his wife. The commentary unpacks each component: 'ownership' (ملك) indicates the purpose of the contract, 'enjoyment' (متعة) refers to the benefits derived from the wife's person, and 'permissibility' (حل) reinforces that this is a lawful right. The author addresses an objection that the definition only mentions the man's enjoyment, noting that the woman also has a right to enjoyment, but the man is specified because he is the one who has the right to enforce it. The contract itself is defined as 'the binding of parts of a legal transaction' (ربط أجزاء التصرف), specifically the offer (إيجاب) and acceptance (قبول) that create the marital bond. The commentary also notes that the word 'nikah' is used metaphorically here to denote the contract, since in legal terminology the contract is what gives rise to the marital relationship. This passage establishes the foundational Hanafi position that marriage is a civil contract with specific legal effects, focusing on the husband's right to enjoyment while acknowledging mutual rights.

Key Terms
النكاح (al-nikāḥ): In this passage, nikāḥ refers to the marriage contract, not the act of intercourse. It is defined as a contract made for ownership of lawful enjoyment.
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut‘ah): Ownership of enjoyment: the husband's exclusive right to derive pleasure from his wife's body (including sexual intimacy) within lawful bounds.
عقد (‘aqd): Contract: the binding of offer (ījāb) and acceptance (qabūl) that creates a legal relationship.
إيجاب (ījāb): Offer: the first statement of one contracting party that initiates the contract. In marriage, it is typically the offer from the woman's guardian or her representative.
قبول (qabūl): Acceptance: the response of the other contracting party that completes the contract. It is closely linked to the offer.
حل (ḥill): Lawful permissibility: the state of being legally allowed. In this context, it describes the nature of the enjoyment granted by the marriage contract.
استمتاع (istimtā‘): Seeking or deriving enjoyment/pleasure. In marriage, it refers to lawful sexual and non-sexual pleasures between spouses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what does 'nikah' primarily refer to?
A) The marriage ceremony and celebration
B) The sexual act between spouses
C) The marriage contract that grants ownership of enjoyment
D) The relationship of love and companionship

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, nikah is defined as a contract that grants the husband lawful ownership of enjoyment from his wife, establishing a civil legal relationship with specific rights and duties.

Definition of Nikah: Contract as Bond and Utterance
Explaining the definition of nikah as consisting of ijab, qabul, and the bond

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three components that constitute the reality of marriage (nikah) in Hanafi law and distinguish between its pillars (arkan) and external conditions (shurut).

Core Idea
Marriage is not merely a bond nor mere words; it is the offer, acceptance, and the resulting bond together.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the precise definition of nikah (marriage) in the Hanafi school. While the term 'aqd (contract) is often used, the reality of marriage comprises three inseparable elements. Understanding this composite nature is essential because it determines which components are integral pillars and which are external conditions, affecting the validity of the marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal and technical meanings of 'aqd
Recognize that 'aqd literally means binding, but in the definition of nikah it refers to the specific bond (irribat) resulting from the utterances.
2
Analyze the three components of nikah
Extract from the passage that the reality of nikah consists of three parts: al-ijab (offer), al-qabul (acceptance), and al-irribat (the bond).
The bond is distinct from the utterances; it is the legal connection created by them.
3
Differentiate between pillars (arkan) and conditions (shurut)
Note that ijab and qabul are pillars (arkan), which are integral parts of the contract. In contrast, conditions like witnesses are external; if a pillar is missing, the contract is invalid (batil).
This distinction is key to Hanafi usul: pillars are essential, conditions are external prerequisites.
4
Understand the proof that ijab and qabul are pillars
The author argues that because the Lawgiver (Shari‘) treats ijab and qabul as pillars, they must be part of the reality of nikah, not external. A pillar is part of the thing (juz’ minhu).
This is a logical inference: if they were external, they would not be called pillars.

From the woman. (2) So the contract: it is the binding of the parts of the transaction: that is, offer and acceptance, legally. But here what is meant by the contract is the result of the infinitive. (1) The permissibility of a man's enjoyment of the woman similarly benefits the permissibility of the woman's enjoyment of the man. The response: the mention of the man is either because he is more honorable than the woman, or because he is the owner of the right over her, even though permissibility of enjoyment is from both sides. For he has the right to compel her to intercourse if she refuses without a legal impediment, whereas she does not have the right to compel him after he has had intercourse with her once, even though that may be obligatory upon him sometimes in terms of religious duty. (1) His saying: 'from the woman'; i.e., the one whom no legal impediment prevents her marriage. So excluded is a male for a male, and a hermaphrodite absolutely, and a jinnī for a human, and whatever women are eternally prohibited. Thus in al-'Ināyah. (2) His saying: 'so the contract'; an explanation of the definition of the contract and what is intended by it in the definition of marriage. (3) His saying: 'that is, offer and acceptance'; offer: it is affirmation linguistically; it is used to name the first speech of one of the two contracting parties, and the speech of the other that is connected to it is called acceptance. [4] His saying: 'but here... etc.'; he intends by it that although the contract originally means binding, (1) what is meant by the contract that results from the infinitive is the connection of the parts of the legal transaction, rather the connected parts such as 'I married' and 'I have been married' and likewise 'I sold' and 'I have bought'. For the Lawgiver has made some declarative sentences performative, such that when they occur, a legal meaning comes into existence which entails a legal ruling. For example, when one says 'I married' and 'I have been married', a legal meaning comes into existence which is marriage, entailing a legal ruling which is ownership of enjoyment. Likewise when one says 'I sold' and 'I have bought', a legal meaning comes into existence which is sale, entailing a legal ruling which is ownership of dominion. Since there is a strong relationship between the performative word and its meaning such that the meaning is never absent from it—because performative speech is the creation of a meaning with a word that accompanies it in existence—performative expressions are named after their meanings. Thus when sale and marriage are mentioned, what is meant is the offer and acceptance. Therefore, here marriage is applied to the contract, even though the contract is legally established for marriage. The complete discussion is in Durar al-Ḥukkām (1:327). (2) Up to here is mentioned in al-'Ināyah (3:187). (3) This addition is mentioned in al-Baḥr al-Rā'iq (3:83); I have followed it after the words of the author of al-'Ināyah after quoting it. And God knows best. Page 7: The Book of Marriage / Its Definition, Ruling, Pillar, and Conditions. It is the connection, but marriage is the offer and acceptance together with that connection. We only say this because the Law considers the offer and acceptance to be the pillars of the marriage contract, not external matters like conditions and the like. And I have mentioned in Sharḥ al-Tanqīḥ in 'Chapter of Prohibition': the infinitive, but what is intended here, i.e., in the definition of marriage, is the result of the infinitive, which is the specific connection obtained by one of the two contracting parties connecting his speech with the speech of the other. 1] His saying: 'but marriage... etc.'; he intends by it that although they interpreted marriage as the contract and intended by the contract that specific connection, that is not free from looseness. Or marriage legally is not merely that connection established between spouses, but rather it is expression of the offer and acceptance together with the connection obtained by connecting one with the other. They are only approximate in the interpretation because of the complete involvement of the contract in the sense of connection in it, so they made it identical. Likewise. [2] His saying: 'We only say this'; i.e., marriage: it is the offer and acceptance together with that connection. [3] His saying: 'because the Law... etc.'; its summary: if marriage legally were exactly that connection resulting from linking the offer and acceptance, then the offer and acceptance themselves would be among the matters external to the reality of marriage, but it is not so. For the offer and acceptance are considered legally among the pillars of marriage, and the pillar of a thing is part of it. So it is known from this that the reality of marriage is composed of the offer, acceptance, and the connection, and it is not merely the connection, nor merely the offer and acceptance. [4] His saying: 'is considered'; in the form of the active present tense, and its attribution to the Law is figurative, for it is a divine institution. The consideration is an action of the Lawgiver, not the Law. It is possible that by 'Law' the Lawgiver is meant metaphorically. [5] His saying: 'like conditions'; which the existence of the conditioned thing depends on, and they are external to its reality. [6] His saying: 'in Sharḥ al-Tanqīḥ'; it is a book by the commentator in the science of Uṣūl al-Fiqh which he named Tanqīḥ al-Uṣūl, then he commented on it and named it al-Tawḍīḥ fī Ḥall Ghavāmiḍ al-Tanqīḥ.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is from the Hanafi fiqh commentary 'Umda al-Ri'aya' on 'Sharh al-Wiqaya' and deals with the definition of marriage (nikah). The author begins by clarifying the term 'aqd (contract) in the context of marriage. He states that 'aqd literally means binding the parts of a transaction, i.e., the offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) as per Islamic law. However, he notes that in the definition of marriage, 'aqd is intended in the sense of the result of the infinitive (al-masdari), meaning the specific bond (irribat) that arises from the offer and acceptance. The author then discusses a side point about the mention of 'man' in earlier texts, explaining that it is because the man is the primary holder of the right to enforce consummation, even though both spouses benefit from the marriage. The passage then delves into the core definition: marriage is not merely the bond (irribat) but consists of the offer and acceptance together with that bond. The evidence for this is that Islamic law considers ijab and qabul as pillars (arkan) of the marriage contract, and a pillar is an integral part of the thing, not an external condition. Therefore, the reality of nikah is composite: it includes both the utterances and the resulting connection. The author also references other works like al-'Inayah and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, and mentions his own work on usul al-fiqh, 'Sharh al-Tanqih'. This passage is a careful Hanafi analysis distinguishing between the literal meaning of 'aqd as binding and the technical definition of nikah, ensuring that students understand that the offer and acceptance are essential components, not external preconditions.

Key Terms
عقد ('aqd): Here it means the specific bond (irribat) resulting from the offer and acceptance in a marriage contract, though literally it means binding.
إيجاب وقبول (ijab wa qabul): Offer and acceptance: the two utterances that constitute the pillars of the marriage contract. Ijab is the first speech (e.g., 'zawwajtuka'), qabul is the second (e.g., 'qabiltu').
ركن (rukn): Pillar: an integral part of the marriage contract without which it is invalid. The passage states that ijab and qabul are arkan, thus they are part of the essence, not external conditions.
ارتباط (irribat): The bond or connection resulting from the linking of offer and acceptance; it is the third component of the reality of nikah according to the commentator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the Hanafi definition of marriage (nikah)?
A) The contract ('aqd) alone
B) The bond (irribat) alone
C) Offer, acceptance, and the resulting bond
D) Offer and acceptance only

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, marriage (nikah) is a composite reality consisting of the offer (ijab), acceptance (qabul), and the resulting bond (irribat); the first two are pillars integral to the contract, not external conditions.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Definition of Nikah: A Composite of Offer, Acceptance, and Legal Connection
Expounding the legal definition of nikah as composite of offer, acceptance, and binding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of nikah and distinguish its three components: ijab, qabul, and irtibat.

Core Idea
Nikah is not merely a marital bond; it is the composite of offer, acceptance, and legal connection.

In this lesson we examine the precise Hanafi legal definition of nikah (marriage) as presented in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The passage clarifies that nikah is not simply the relationship between spouses, but a contract composed of three integral elements: the offer (ijab), the acceptance (qabul), and the legal connection (irtibat) that binds them. Understanding this composite nature is essential for analyzing the structure of marriage and other bilateral contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'nikah' and note the author's statement that it is a composite of three elements. This is the starting point of the definition.
The author explicitly says 'nikah is the offer and acceptance with that connection'.
2
Distinguish between pillars and conditions
Recognize that ijab and qabul are classified as pillars (arkan), not external conditions (shurut). Pillars are intrinsic parts of the reality of nikah, while conditions are external prerequisites.
The text contrasts arkan with shurut to emphasize that ijab and qabul belong to the essence of the contract.
3
Understand the role of irtibat (legal connection)
The legal connection is the bond that arises when the offer and acceptance are linked in a manner recognized by the Lawgiver. It is not a separate act but the effect of the proper linkage.
Irtibat is described as 'the specific connection obtained by binding the speech of one contractor to the other'.
4
Apply the analogy with sale (bay')
The author draws a parallel between nikah and sale to illustrate that both contracts share the same tripartite structure: offer, acceptance, and legal connection.
In sale, the legal meaning (ownership) results from the composite, not from the offer or acceptance alone.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab nuance
Note that the Hanafi insistence on including the pillars in the definition opposes a more simplified view that reduces the contract to the legal effect alone.
The text refutes the claim that sale is merely the legal meaning with ijab and qabul as instruments.

وهو الارتباط، لكن النكاح هو الإيجاب والقبولُ مع ذلك الإرتباط وإنما قلنا هذا ؛ لأنَّ الشرع " يعتبر الإيجاب والقبول ؛ لأنهما أركان عقد النكاح ، لا أمورٌ خارجية كالشرائط ونحوها.
وقد ذكرتُ في «شرح التنقيح» في «فصل النهي»: المصدري ، لكن المراد به هاهنا أي في تعريف النكاح هو الحاصل بالمصدر، وهو الارتباط الخاص الحاصل بربط أحد المتعاقدين كلامه بالآخر.
[١] قوله : لكن النكاح ... الخ ؛ يريد به أنهم وإن فسروا النكاح بالعقد وأرادوا بالعقد ذلك الارتباط الخاص، لكن لا يخلو ذلك عن تسامح، أو النكاح شرعاً ليس مجرد ذلك الارتباط الثابت بين الزوجين، بل هو عبارة عن الإيجاب والقبول مع الارتباط الذي حصل بربط أحدهما مع الآخر، وإنما تسامحوا في التفسير لكمال مدخلية العقد بمعنى الارتباط فيه فجعلوه عينه.

We only say this because the Law considers the offer and acceptance, as they are the pillars of the marriage contract, not external matters like conditions and the like. I have mentioned in Sharh al-Tanqih, in the Chapter on Prohibition: the infinitive, but what is meant here, i.e. in the definition of nikah, is that which is obtained by the infinitive, which is the specific connection obtained by binding the speech of one of the two contractors to the other. [1] His statement: 'But nikah ... etc.'; he intends by it that although they interpreted nikah as the contract and by contract they meant that specific connection, that is not free from leniency. Or nikah in sacred law is not merely that connection established between the spouses, but rather it is the expression of offer and acceptance with the connection that occurred by binding one of them with the other. They were lenient in the interpretation due to the complete intervention of the contract in the sense of connection in it, so they made it identical. Likewise. [2] His statement: 'We only say this'; i.e. nikah: it is offer and acceptance with that connection. [3] His statement: 'Because the Law ... etc.'; its summary: if nikah in sacred law were an expression of the very connection based on the binding of offer and acceptance, then the very offer and acceptance would be matters external to the reality of nikah, but it is not so, because offer and acceptance are considered in sacred law as pillars of nikah, and the pillar of a thing is part of it. So it is known from this that the reality of nikah is composed of offer, acceptance, and connection, and it is not an expression of mere connection, nor of mere offer and acceptance. [4] His statement: 'is considered' in the form of the active present tense, attributing it to the Law, and it is an expression of a divine placing metaphorically, because consideration is the act of the Lawgiver, not the Law. It is possible that by 'the Law' the Lawgiver is meant metaphorically. [5] His statement: 'like conditions' which the existence of the conditioned depends on, and they are external to its reality. [6] His statement: 'in Sharh al-Tanqih'; it is a book by the commentator in the science of Usul al-Fiqh which he named Tanqih al-Usul, then he commented on it and named it al-Tawdih fi Hall Ghawamid al-Tanqih. [PAGE_008] [1] Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah: sale, for the Law rules that the offer and acceptance existing sensually are connected by a legal connection, so a legal meaning is obtained which is the ownership of the buyer as its effect. That meaning is sale. So what is meant by that meaning is the composite of offer and acceptance with that legal connection, not that sale is merely that legal meaning, and offer and acceptance are its instrument, as some imagined. [11] His statement: 'like sale ... etc.'; the full wording in al-Tanqih is: 'The prohibition is either regarding sensory things: like adultery and drinking wine, and it implies ugliness in itself unanimously unless through evidence that the prohibition is for the ugliness of something else; and regarding legal things: like fasting and sale ... etc.' And he said in al-Tawdih: 'What is meant by sensory things is what has only sensory existence. What is meant by legal things is what has legal existence along with sensory existence, like sale, for it has sensory existence, since offer and acceptance exist sensually, and along with this sensory existence it has legal existence, for the Law rules that offer and acceptance existing sensually are connected by a legal connection, so a legal meaning is obtained which is the ownership of the buyer as its effect. So that meaning is sale, so that if offer and acceptance occur in a non-place, the Law does not consider it sale; and if they occur with option, the Law rules the existence of sale without ownership following upon it, so the legal existence is established.' End. [2] His statement: 'So what is meant ... etc.'; means that what is meant by the legal meaning that occurs after the connection of offer and acceptance is the composite of offer and acceptance with the legal connection between them. So sale is an expression in sacred law for that composite, not for the very offer and acceptance alone, nor for that connection alone. Similarly, nikah is not an expression for one of these things, but for the composite of three. So offer and acceptance are included in the realities of these contracts whose existence depends on them in sacred law. [3] His statement: 'as some imagined'; it is attached to the negated. For some claimed that sale in sacred law...
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah provides a detailed legal definition of nikah (marriage) as a contract in the Hanafi school. The author begins by noting that the literal meaning of nikah is 'connection', but the shar'i definition is more precise: it is the combination of ijab (offer) and qabul (acceptance) together with the legal connection (irtibat) that binds them. The reason for this composite definition is that Islamic law considers ijab and qabul as the pillars (arkan) of the marriage contract, not external conditions (shurut). Pillars are integral parts of the contract, so they must be included in its definition. The author then references his earlier work 'Sharh al-Tanqih' to clarify that the connection intended is the specific legal bond resulting from the binding of one contractor's speech to the other. The commentary then expands on the base text (presumably from 'Sharh al-Wiqayah') by explaining each phrase: why the definition cannot be reduced to just the connection (it would exclude the pillars), why ijab and qabul are not external (they are pillars, hence parts of the reality), and a linguistic note on the word 'yu'tabaru' (is considered) attributing the act to the Lawgiver. The passage then draws an analogy with sale (bay') to illustrate the composite nature of legal contracts. Just as sale is not merely the legal meaning of ownership but the composite of ijab, qabul, and the legal connection, so too is nikah. The author refutes an imagined view that sale is merely the legal meaning with ijab and qabul as mere instruments. Thus, the Hanafi position is clear: the reality of nikah—and all bilateral contracts—is a composite of offer, acceptance, and the legal connection that arises between them.

Key Terms
نكاح (nikah): In this passage, nikah is defined as the composite of ijab (offer), qabul (acceptance), and the legal connection (irtibat) between them.
إيجاب (ijab): The offer made by one of the contracting parties (usually the guardian or the husband) in a marriage contract.
قبول (qabul): The acceptance of the offer by the other party (usually the wife or her representative).
ارتباط (irtibat): The legal connection (a legally recognized bond) that arises between the offer and acceptance, forming the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition presented in this passage, what does the reality of nikah (marriage) comprise?
A) The legal connection (irtibat) alone.
B) The offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) alone.
C) The offer, acceptance, and the legal connection together.
D) The offer, acceptance, and the legal connection, plus external conditions.

Key Takeaway
Nikah is a composite contract: ijab and qabul are its pillars, and irtibat is the legal connection binding them into a single reality.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

The Definition of Marriage and Its Four Causes
Defining the contract as a composite of offer, acceptance, and legal connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of the marriage contract as a composite of offer, acceptance, and legal connection, and identify its four causes (efficient, material, formal, final).

Core Idea
A valid contract is a composite of offer, acceptance, and the legal connection established by Shari'ah.

This passage delves into the true nature of contracts like sale and marriage in Hanafi fiqh. Using the framework of four causes, the author demonstrates that the offer and acceptance are not mere instruments but integral pillars of the contract. Understanding this definition is essential for grasping the contractual basis of marriage in the Hanafi school and avoiding a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Debate
Recognize that there is a disagreement about the reality of a contract: some claim it is only the resulting legal meaning, while others (the correct Hanafi view) hold it is the composite of offer, acceptance, and the legal connection.
The false view treats offer and acceptance as external tools.
2
Distinguish Sensory and Legal Existence
Understand that a contract has both sensory existence (the spoken words) and legal existence (the ruling of Shari'ah linking them). The legal existence depends on the Lawgiver's consideration.
Offer and acceptance are sensory; the connection is legal and non-sensory.
3
Apply the Four Causes
Analyze the contract using the four Aristotelian causes: efficient cause (the parties), material cause (the offer and acceptance), formal cause (the legal connection), final cause (the resulting legal meaning, e.g., ownership or marriage).
These causes together constitute the full reality of the contract.
4
Refute the Wrong View
See why the false view fails: if the contract were only the final cause, it would lack material and formal causes, making it an incomplete composite. The offer and acceptance must be internal parts (pillars) for the contract to be a composite.
The refutation proves that offer and acceptance are essential, not optional.

Sale: The Shari'ah rules that the offer and acceptance that exist sensibly are connected by a legal connection, so a legal meaning is obtained, which is the ownership of the buyer as its effect. That meaning is the sale. What is intended by that meaning is the composite of the offer and acceptance together with that legal connection; not that sale is merely that legal meaning, and the offer and acceptance are its instrument, as some wrongly imagined. Along with this sensory existence it has legal existence, for the Shari'ah rules that the offer and acceptance that exist sensibly are connected by a legal connection, so a legal meaning is obtained, which is the ownership of the buyer as its effect. That meaning is the sale, so that if offer and acceptance occur in the wrong place, the Shari'ah does not consider it a sale; and if they occur with an option, the Shari'ah rules the existence of the sale without ownership following, thus legal existence is established. That is a false claim, for if that were the case, the offer and acceptance would not be counted among the pillars, which are the matters included in the reality of a thing, while it is not so, for they are counted among the pillars of the contract. If it were an expression of only that meaning, it would not have a material and formal cause, because they are characteristics of composite realities. He said: 'the efficient cause' is that from which the action proceeds. He said: 'the material cause' is that from which a thing is composed and constituted, and it exists potentially, and that which necessitates its actual existence is the formal cause, which subsists in the material cause so the composite is constituted. He said: 'which is considered' is an indication that that connection is not sensory existence, but rather an intellectual, legal meaning that exists by the consideration and ruling of the Lawgiver. Without the consideration of the Lawgiver, it would have no existence at all. Likewise, what it is composed of are legal matters, and it is contrary to the pattern of sensory matters like drinking wine, adultery, and the like. He said: 'the final cause' is that which motivates the agent to his action. It is only in voluntary actions, and it is mentally prior to the existence of the effect, and existentially posterior to it.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of contracts (specifically sale and marriage) in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that a contract has both a sensory and a legal existence. The sensory part is the offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) uttered by the parties. The legal part is the connection (irtibat) that the Shari'ah establishes between them, which results in a legal meaning (ma'na shar'i) such as ownership in sale or marital rights in marriage. The author refutes the view of some who claim that only this legal meaning is the contract, and that the offer and acceptance are merely instruments external to its reality. Instead, the contract is the composite (majmu' murakkab) of all three: offer, acceptance, and the legal connection. To prove this, the author introduces the four causes (al-'ilal al-arba') analogous to Aristotelian causality: (1) efficient cause (al-'illa al-fa'iliyya): the two contracting parties; (2) material cause (al-'illa al-madiyya): the offer and acceptance; (3) formal cause (al-'illa al-suriya): the legal connection that the Lawgiver considers; (4) final cause (al-'illa al-gha'iyya): the legal meaning (e.g., ownership or marriage). If the contract were only the final cause, it would lack a material and formal cause, which are essential for a composite reality. Therefore, offer and acceptance are pillars (arkan) of the contract, integral to its reality. This analysis applies equally to sale and marriage.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): The verbal offer made by one contracting party, expressing consent to enter the contract.
قبول (qabul): The verbal acceptance by the other party, matching the offer, forming the contract.
نكاح (nikah): Marriage contract, defined here as the composite of offer, acceptance, and the legal connection established by the Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following correctly describes the marriage contract in Hanafi fiqh?
A) It is the legal meaning (marriage relationship) only; offer and acceptance are external tools.
B) It is the composite of offer, acceptance, and the legal connection established by Shari'ah.
C) It is the verbal expressions of offer and acceptance only.
D) It is the intention of the parties to enter marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines contracts as composites of offer, acceptance, and the Shari'ah-established legal connection; offer and acceptance are pillars, not mere instruments.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
