End. There has been disagreement on the correct view regarding the requirement of understanding. End. And in 'Al-Nahr', what is in 'Al-Khulasa' is interpreted as the view requiring presence without hearing or understanding, i.e., contrary to the soundest as mentioned. And al-Rahmati reconciled by interpreting the view requiring understanding as requiring understanding that it is a marriage contract, and the view not requiring it as not requiring understanding of the meanings of the words after understanding that what is intended is the marriage contract. (5) And according to al-Shafi'i, justice ('adala) is required, but the Shaykh of the Shafi'is in Iraq, our teacher Abd al-Karim al-Mudarris, may Allah have mercy on him, said in his beneficial book 'Al-Anwar al-Qudsiya fi al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyya' (p. 7): 'It should be known that Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, has a position regarding the testimony of sinful witnesses, and also has a position regarding the guardianship of a sinful person, and a large group of scholars of his school whose taqlid is permitted chose this position.' (7)
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: the validity of marriage when witnesses are sinful (fasiq), and the requirement that witnesses understand the contract. The commentator explains that the marriage is valid with two sinful witnesses because a fasiq has guardianship over himself as a Muslim, and therefore has a deficient guardianship over others, which is sufficient for the contract to be valid, even if it would not be sufficient for the judge to rule based on their testimony. This is the Hanafi position. The passage then cites a disagreement: Abu Yusuf and Abu Sahl held that if the same session is continuous, the contract is valid by istihsan, but if the session is separate, it is not valid by consensus. However, the relied-upon position (as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar) is that it is valid even if the witnesses are sinful. The second issue is understanding (fahm). Some Hanafi authorities required that the witnesses understand the language of the contracting parties, while others held that it is not necessary. The passage reconciles these views by distinguishing between understanding that it is a marriage contract (which is required) and understanding the precise meanings of the words (which is not required). The text then notes that al-Shafi'i requires the witnesses to be just ('adl), but later Shafi'i scholars have a position that allows sinful witnesses, and some follow that. However, the Hanafi position is clear: fasiq witnesses do not invalidate the contract.

Key Terms
فاسق (fāsiq): A sinful person; one who commits major sins or persists in minor sins.
عدالة (ʿadāla): Justice; being upright and avoiding major sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what is the ruling on a marriage conducted in the presence of two sinful (fasiq) witnesses?
A) The marriage is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The marriage is valid, but the witnesses cannot testify in court.
C) The marriage is valid only if the witnesses understand Arabic.
D) The marriage is suspended pending their repentance.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the marriage contract is valid with fasiq witnesses; the Shafi'i school requires just witnesses.

Nikah — Marriage

Do Marriage Witnesses Need to Understand the Contract? Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the requirement of witness understanding in marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi views on witness understanding and apply the reconciled position.

Core Idea
Witnesses must understand that it is a marriage, but not every linguistic detail.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a dispute on whether marriage witnesses must understand the language of the contract. Some require full understanding, others only require presence. The passage reconciles these views: witnesses must understand that a marriage contract is taking place, but they need not grasp the exact meaning of every word. This lesson will clarify the positions and their practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting views
One view requires witnesses to understand the contract (language and terms). Another view (the soundest in some texts) does not require understanding.
The source mentions that in 'Al-Khulasa', the soundest opinion is that the contract is valid without understanding.
2
Examine the reconciliation
The commentator reconciles by saying: those who require understanding mean understanding that it is a marriage; those who do not require it mean that once witnesses understand it is a marriage, they don't need to understand the precise words.
This distinction is attributed to al-Rahmati.
3
Apply to a scenario
Consider a marriage conducted in Arabic with two non-Arabic-speaking witnesses. Under the reconciled view, if they know it is a marriage, it is valid. If they have no idea what is happening, it is not valid.
4
Note the relied-upon position
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) as per the commentator is that understanding that it is a marriage is required, but not understanding of linguistic details.
This is supported by the fact that most matn books except 'Al-Tanwir' did not mention the understanding requirement.

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: the validity of marriage when witnesses are sinful (fasiq), and the requirement that witnesses understand the contract. Some Hanafi authorities required that the witnesses understand the language of the contracting parties, while others held that it is not necessary. The passage reconciles these views by distinguishing between understanding that it is a marriage contract (which is required) and understanding the precise meanings of the words (which is not required). The text then notes that al-Shafi'i requires the witnesses to be just ('adl), but later Shafi'i scholars have a position that allows sinful witnesses, and some follow that. However, the Hanafi position is clear: fasiq witnesses do not invalidate the contract.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Validity of Marriage with Fasiq and Qadhf-Punished Witnesses
Explains validity of marriage with fasiq, qadhf-punished, blind, and son witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage with fasiq and qadhf-punished witnesses, explain the dalil from Quran 24:4, and distinguish between judicial testimony and marriage presence.

Core Idea
Marriage is valid with fasiq witnesses because the Quranic rejection of testimony is for court, not for marriage.

The Hanafi school takes a lenient position on who can witness a marriage. While other schools require uprightness (ʿadālah), the Hanafis hold that even a fasiq (sinner) or a person flogged for false accusation (qadhf) can serve as a witness. This ruling is based on a key distinction between shahādah (court testimony) and ḥuḍūr (presence) at a marriage contract. In this lesson, we will explore the reasoning behind this position and how it is supported by the Quran.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Rulings
From the passage, note that marriage is sahīḥ (valid) with fasiq witnesses, with those punished for qadhf, with blind witnesses, and with sons of the spouses. Recognize the main hukm.
The passage also mentions a dispute about whether witnesses must understand the contract.
2
2. Analyze the Dalīl
The passage cites Quran 24:4 which says 'accept not their testimony ever' for qadhf. Understand that the Hanafi interpretation limits this to judicial testimony.
The key is the word 'shahādah' in the verse.
3
3. Understand the Distinction
Grasp that witnessing a marriage (ishhād) is not shahādah but ḥuḍūr for iʿlān (publicity). Thus the Quranic verse does not apply.
This resolves the apparent contradiction that Bukhari raised.
4
4. Examine the Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilāf
Abu Yusuf and Abu Sahl held that if witnesses change in the same sitting, it is valid by istihsān, but if in another gathering, invalid by consensus. Note this nuance.
The mufta bihi position is that marriage is valid even without understanding the contract.
5
5. Compare with Shafiʿi
The Shafiʿi school requires ʿadālah in witnesses. Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf): Hanafi permits, Shafiʿi invalidates without ʿadālah.
Some later Shafiʿis allowed following the Hanafi position in cases of difficulty.

أو محدودين في قذف، وعند أعميين، وابني الزوجين، وابني أحدهما لا من الآخر
[1] قوله: أو محدودين في قذف؛ هو بفتحتين السب بالزنا واتهام غيره به، وحكمه ما ذكره الله في كتابه بقوله: وَالَّذِينَ يَرْمُونَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ ثُمَّ لَمْ يَأْتُوا بِأَرْبَعَةِ شُهَدَاءَ فَاجْلِدُوهُمْ ثَمَنِينَ جَلْدَةً وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا لَمْ شَهَدَةً أَبَدًا (2). فالذي سب أحداً بالزنا ولم يثبته وحد بحد القذف فهو مردود الشهادة وإن تاب وأصلح عملاً صالحاً، فرد شهادته لا لفسقه وإلا لزم أن تقبل بعد توبته، وهو مخالف لقوله: أبدًا. وأيضاً: الفاسق مطلقاً ولو كان فسقه بعمل آخر غير مقبول الشهادة، فما معنى ذكر هذا الحكم في هذه الواقعة، بل هو تتمة جزاء عمله، وهو قذفه لكن النكاح ينعقد بشهادة المحدودين في قذف. واعترض البخاري في «صحيحه» في هذه المسألة: بأن هذا تناقض واضح (3)، فإن عدم قبول شهادة المحدود في القذف مطلقاً والقول بقبولها في النكاح متناقضان. وجوابه: ظاهر على كل ماهر فإن قبولهما أمر آخر، وانعقاد النكاح بحضوره أمر آخر، والمنفي إنما هو القبول عند القاضي لا مجرد الشهادة للانعقاد، فأين هذا من ذلك.
Marriage is valid in the presence of two fasiq (sinful) witnesses, then one of them leaves and another arrives, so they repeat the witnessing in his presence. [Commentary:] It is necessary that the witnesses understand (the contract); if a father contracts for an Arab woman in the presence of two Indians who do not understand their speech, it is not valid. In al-Khulasah, the more correct view is that it is valid without understanding. Some harmonize: those who require understanding mean understanding that it is a marriage contract; those who do not require it mean understanding the words after understanding the intended meaning. Fasiq witnesses have walayah over themselves as Muslims, thus partial walayah over others. Abu Yusuf and Abu Sahl held that if the witnesses change in the same sitting it is valid by istihsan, but if in a different gathering, invalid by consensus. The relied-upon texts do not mention the condition of understanding except in al-Tanwir. Al-Rahmati reconciles: requiring understanding means understanding that it is a marriage; not requiring means understanding the words after knowing the intended meaning. According to al-Shafi'i, 'adalah is required, but some of his followers adopted a position allowing fasiq witnesses. Regarding those punished for qadhf (false accusation of zina): the perpetually rejected testimony applies to judicial testimony, not to witnessing marriage. Bukhari objected that this is a contradiction, but the answer is that acceptance in court is one matter, and the validity of marriage by their presence is another.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on the matn rulings from Al-Wiqayah regarding who may serve as witnesses for a valid marriage. The author begins by affirming that marriage is valid in the presence of two fasiq witnesses—those who commit major sins but are still Muslims. The reason given is that a fasiq has partial walayah (guardianship) over himself, and thus a diminished capacity to witness for others. This is sufficient for the contract's validity, even if it would not suffice in a court of law. A key issue discussed is whether the witnesses must understand the contract. Some scholars require that they understand it is a marriage, while others require understanding the specific words. The relied-upon position in the later Hanafi school is that validity does not require linguistic comprehension of the words, as long as they understand the nature of the contract. The passage then addresses witnesses who have been flogged for qadhf (false accusation of zina). Despite the Quranic verse (24:4) that permanently rejects their testimony, the Hanafi position is that they can still validly witness a marriage. The distinction is clear: the verse refers to judicial testimony (shahadah), while witnessing a marriage is merely presence (hudur) for public announcement. Bukhari's objection is thus answered by clarifying that the Quranic rejection applies only to court testimony. Finally, the text notes that blind witnesses and sons of the spouses also suffice for validity, provided they meet the general conditions. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the purpose of witnesses as i'lan (public notification) rather than probity.

Key Terms
فاسق (fāsiq): A Muslim who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking moral probity but still having legal capacity.
محدود في قذف (maḥdūd fī qadhf): A person who has been flogged for falsely accusing a chaste person of zina (sexual intercourse), as per Quran 24:4.
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony given in court, which requires certain conditions; the text distinguishes it from mere presence (hudur) at a marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man and woman marry in the presence of two witnesses who have been flogged for qadhf (false accusation of zina), is the marriage valid?
A) Yes, the marriage is valid.
B) No, the marriage is invalid because their testimony is permanently rejected.
C) Yes, but only if they repent first.
D) No, because blind witnesses are required instead.

Key Takeaway
Marriage witnesses do not require 'adalah; even fasiq and qadhf-punished individuals suffice for validity because the Quranic rejection of testimony is limited to court proceedings.

In'iqad vs. Ithbat: Validity of Marriage with Rejected Witnesses
Commentary expanding on a ruling with proof and addressing an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between in'iqad (valid formation) and ithbat (judicial proof) in marriage, and apply it to cases involving witnesses whose testimony is permanently rejected.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a marriage can be validly formed with witnesses whose testimony is rejected in court — validity is not dependent on provability.

This lesson examines a crucial Hanafi principle: the difference between the validity of a marriage contract (in'iqad) and its establishment in court (ithbat). The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses the objection of Imam al-Bukhari and clarifies that the presence of witnesses with rejected testimony (such as those punished for qadhf or blind persons) does not invalidate the contract; it only affects the ability to prove the marriage in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (marriage in the presence of witnesses whose testimony is permanently rejected) and what the Hanafi ruling is (sahih/invalid).
The act is the contract of marriage (nikah) with specific witnesses.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary textual evidence cited: Quran 24:4 regarding the permanent rejection of testimony for those punished for qadhf.
The verse is the core proof but its interpretation is key.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the Hanafi school interprets the verse: the prohibition 'do not accept their testimony ever' applies only to judicial acceptance (qabul), not to mere presence (hudur) for contract formation.
This is the critical interpretive move that distinguishes in'iqad from ithbat.
4
Analyse the objection (al-Bukhari)
Understand the opposing view: that the absolute language of the verse should forbid even the use of such witnesses for the marriage contract itself, creating a contradiction.
Al-Bukhari's objection represents a different usul approach.
5
State the Hanafi response and its implication
The Hanafi response: the negation is only for acceptance before a judge; the contract is valid even if it cannot be proven in court by those witnesses. This leads to the general principle that marriage has two rulings (in'iqad and ithbat) with different requirements for witnesses.
This distinction is unique to marriage compared to other contracts.

أو محدودين في قذف ، وعند أعميين، وابني الزوجين، وابني أحدهما لا من الآخر
[١] قوله : أو محدودين في قذف ؛ هو بفتحتين السب بالزنا واتهـام غـيـره بـه ، وحكمه ما ذكره الله الله في كتابه بقوله : وَالَّذِينَ يَرْمُونَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ ثُمَّ لَمْ يَأْتُوا بِأَرْبَعَةِ شُهَدَاءَ فَاجْلِدُوهُمْ ثَمَنِينَ جَلْدَةً وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا لَمْ شَهَدَةً أَبَدًا ) (٢) .
فالذي سب أحداً بالزنا ولم يثبته وحد بحد القذف فهو مردود الشهادة وإن تاب وأصلح عملاً صالحاً، فرد شهادته لا لفسقه وإلا لزم أن تقبل بعد توبته، وهو مخالف لقوله : أبدًا. وأيضاً : الفاسق مطلقاً ولو كان فسقه بعمل آخر غير مقبول الشهادة ، فما معنى ذكر هذا الحكم في هذه الواقعة، بل هو تتمة جزاء عمله، وهو قذفه لكن النكاح ينعقد بشهادة المحدودين في قذف. واعترض البخاري في «صحيحه» في هذه المسألة : بأن هذا تناقض واضح (۳)، فإن
عدم قبول شهادة المحدود في القذف مطلقاً والقول بقبولها في النكاح متناقضان. وجوابه : ظاهر على كل ماهر فإن قبولهما أمر آخر، وانعقاد النكاح بحضوره أمر آخر، والمنفي إنما هو القبول عند القاضي لا مجرد الشهادة للانعقاد، فأين هذا من
ذلك.

لكن لا يظهرُ بهما إن ادعى القريب ، أي إذا نكحا بحضور ابني الزوج ، فإن ادعى هو لم تقبل شهادة ابنيه له ، أما إذا ادعت المرأة تقبل شهادتهما لها ، وإن نكحها عند ابني الزوجة، فإن ادعت لا تقبل شهادتهما لها ، وإن ادعى الزوج تقبل له.
فالنكاح ينعقد بحضورهما لكونهما من أرباب الولاية، وإن لم يظهر ذلك عند القاضي، ومثله شهادة الأعميين، فإن النكاح ينعقد بحضورهما ؛ لعدم قصور سماعهما، ولا تقبل شهادتهما عند الأداء، وكذا شهادة ابن الزوجة من زوج سابق، أو ابن الزوج من زوجة أخرى، فإن بحضوره ينعقد النكاح ولا يثبت به في حق القريب، ونظائر هذه المسألة كثيرة على المهرة غير خفية).
[١] قوله : لكن لا يظهر ؛ وذلك لأن النكاح له حكمان، حكم الانعقاد، وحكم الاظهار والثبوتُ عند التنازع والشهادة مشروطة فيه لكليهما، بخلاف سائر العقود، فإنّ الشهادة إنما تلزم فيها للحكم الثاني دون الأول، فعند الإظهار لا تقبل فيه أيضاً شهادة من لا تقبل شهادته في سائر المعاملات ومن ثمّ يجوز انعقاده بحضرة العبدين، ولا يظهر ذلك بشهادتهما عند التجاحد. (٢) قوله : إن ادعى القريب ؛ وأما إذا ادعى غيره وأنكر القريب تقبل شهادته لما تقرر أن شهادة الابن لا تقبل للأب والأم ، وتقبلُ عليهما لوجود التهمة في الأول وانتفائه في الثاني.
Or those who have been punished for false accusation of adultery (qadhf), and in the presence of two blind men, and the two sons of the husband and wife, and the son of one of them not from the other. [1] His saying: 'Or those who have been punished for qadhf' – it is, with two fatah, reviling by (accusation of) adultery and accusing another of it. Its ruling is what Allah, may Allah be exalted, mentioned in His Book: 'And those who accuse chaste women and then do not bring four witnesses – lash them with eighty lashes and do not accept their testimony ever.' So whoever reviles someone by (accusation of) adultery and does not prove it, and is punished with the punishment of qadhf, his testimony is rejected even if he repents and does righteous deeds. The rejection of his testimony is not due to his transgression (fisq), otherwise it would be necessary to accept it after his repentance, and that would contradict His saying: 'ever'. Also, the outright transgressor (fasiq), even if his transgression is by another (sinful) act, is rejected in testimony. So what is the meaning of mentioning this ruling in this particular case? Rather, it is a completion of the punishment for his act, which is his qadhf. But marriage is contracted by the testimony of those who have been punished for qadhf. Al-Bukhari objected in his Sahih concerning this issue, saying that this is a clear contradiction, for the absolute non-acceptance of the testimony of the one punished for qadhf and the assertion of its acceptance in marriage are contradictory. Its answer is obvious to every skilled (scholar): for their acceptance is another matter, and the contracting of marriage in their presence is another matter. What is negated is only acceptance before the judge, not mere testimony for contracting. So where is this from that? However, it is not established (i.e., in court) by them if a relative claims (contradicts). That is, if they marry in the presence of the two sons of the husband, if he claims, the testimony of his two sons for him is not accepted. But if the woman claims, their testimony for her is accepted. If he marries her in the presence of the two sons of the wife, if she claims, their testimony for her is not accepted, and if the husband claims, it is accepted for him. So the marriage is contracted by their presence because they are among those who have (right of) guardianship (arbab al-wilaya), even if that is not established (i.e., in court) before the judge. Similarly is the testimony of two blind men – the marriage is contracted by their presence due to the absence of deficiency in their hearing, but their testimony is not accepted at the time of performance (before the judge). Likewise, the testimony of the son of the wife from a previous husband, or the son of the husband from another wife – by his presence the marriage is contracted but it is not established (in court) regarding the relative. Examples of this issue are many, not hidden to the skilled. [1] His saying: 'However, it is not established (in court) by them' – that is because marriage has two rulings: the ruling of contracting (in'iqad) and the ruling of manifestation and establishment (ithbat) at the time of dispute. Testimony is required for both, unlike other contracts, for testimony is only required for the second ruling, not the first. So at the time of manifestation (in court) the testimony of one whose testimony is not accepted in other transactions is also not accepted here. Hence, it is permissible for it to be contracted in the presence of two slaves, but it is not established (in court) by their testimony at the time of denial. (2) His saying: 'if a relative claims' – but if someone else claims and the relative denies, his testimony is accepted because it is established that the testimony of a son is not accepted for (the benefit of) his father and mother, but is accepted against them due to the existence of suspicion (tuhma) in the former and its absence in the latter.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Al-Wiqaya, expanding the ruling on which individuals can serve as witnesses for the validity (in'iqad) of a marriage contract, as distinct from the establishment (ithbat) of that marriage in court. The author first explains the term 'those punished for qadhf' – it refers to a person who has falsely accused another of adultery and received the prescribed punishment. He cites the Quranic verse (24:4) which permanently rejects their testimony. The commentator clarifies that this rejection is not because of their transgression (fisq), but as a specific punishment for qadhf. This is proven by the fact that if it were due to fisq, the testimony would be accepted after repentance, but the verse says 'ever'. Then the author addresses an objection from Imam al-Bukhari in his Sahih: how can the testimony of such a person be rejected absolutely, yet be accepted for the validity of marriage? The answer is a key distinction of Hanafi fiqh: acceptance (qabul) of testimony for judgment before a judge is different from mere presence (hudur) for the contract's validity. The permanent rejection applies only to the former; the latter is not negated. The commentator then elaborates on various cases: (1) If two sons of the husband witness the marriage, the marriage is valid (in contract) but the husband cannot prove it in court using their testimony (since sons' testimony is not accepted for their father). The wife, however, can use their testimony against the husband because there is no tuhma (suspicion) in testifying for a non-relative. (2) Similarly for two blind persons: their hearing is not deficient, so the marriage is valid in their presence, but they cannot testify in court due to lack of sight. (3) A son from a previous marriage (stepson) can also validly witness the contract but not establish it in court for his parent. The commentator then states a general principle: marriage has two rulings – the ruling of contracting (in'iqad) and the ruling of establishment (ithbat) at the time of dispute. Testimony is required for both, but the requirement differs. For the first, anyone who has the capacity to consent (i.e., free, sane, pubescent) can be a witness; for the second, only those who are admissible as witnesses in court (upright, etc.) can testify. This is specific to marriage, unlike other contracts where testimony is only required for establishment. Finally, he mentions that even the presence of slaves makes the marriage contract valid (though slaves cannot marry themselves), but their testimony cannot establish it in court. The passage thus guards against confusing the validity of the contract with its provability.

Key Terms
محدود في قذف (muhadd fī qadhf): A person who has been legally punished for falsely accusing another of adultery (zina) without bringing four witnesses.
انعقاد (in'iqad): The valid formation of a contract, specifically regarding its essential elements (arkan) and conditions, as opposed to its legal effect in court.
إظهار (izhār): The manifestation or establishment of a contract in a judicial setting, i.e., proving it before a judge with admissible evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a marriage contract valid (in'iqad) when concluded in the presence of a person punished for qadhf, even though their testimony is permanently rejected in court?
A) Because the person's testimony is accepted for marriage despite the Quranic verse due to istihsan.
B) Because the rejection of testimony only applies to judicial acceptance (qabul), not to the presence (hudur) required for the contract's formation.
C) Because the verse was abrogated by later revelation regarding marriage.
D) Because such a person is not considered a fasiq, so his testimony is valid for all purposes.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the validity of a marriage contract (in'iqad) and its provability in court (ithbat); witnesses whose testimony is permanently rejected can still validly witness the contract, but cannot prove it in court.

The Validity of Dhimmī Witnesses in a Muslim-Dhimmī Marriage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of Dhimmī witnesses in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a Muslim's marriage to a Dhimmī woman when witnessed by two Dhimmī men, and explain the reasoning behind the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
When a Muslim marries a Dhimmī woman, two Dhimmī witnesses suffice because witnesses in marriage honor human dignity, not financial rights.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school about the conditions for valid witnesses in a marriage between a Muslim man and a Dhimmī (protected non-Muslim) woman. The central question is whether the marriage is valid when the two witnesses are also Dhimmī, given the general rule that non-Muslim testimony against a Muslim is not accepted. The Hanafi masters differ, and we will explore their arguments and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the two Hanafi positions
Identify the two differing opinions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the marriage valid; Muhammad and Zufar hold it invalid. Note that the matn declares it valid, which is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah position.
The dissenting opinion of Muhammad and Zufar is a minority view within the school.
2
Analyse the dissenting proof
Muhammad and Zufar argue that no testimony of a non-Muslim against a Muslim is ever accepted, so Dhimmī witnesses cannot testify against a Muslim husband. This would make the marriage invalid because the witnesses are unreliable.
Their proof is based on the general rule of testimony: a non-Muslim cannot bear witness against a Muslim.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf distinguish the role of witnesses in marriage from that in financial transactions. In marriage, witnesses are required for the dignity of the human being (milk al-mut'ah), not for financial rights. Since Dhimmī have authority over their fellow Dhimmī, their testimony suffices for the woman.
The analogy to sale (where witnesses are not required) is rejected because marriage involves human dignity.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the marriage is valid and requires no renewal; according to Muhammad and Zufar, it is invalid and a new contract with Muslim witnesses is needed.
This difference directly affects the validity of existing marriages.
5
Identify the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as cited in Fath al-Qadeer. This is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah and the majority view within the school.
The mufta bihi is that the marriage is valid with two Dhimmī witnesses.

:
أي المسلم، فإن شهادة الكافر على المسلم لا تقبل، وإن ادعى المسلم تقبل له [1] قوله : كما صح ؛ هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، وخالف فيه محمد وزفر ؛ لأنه لا شهادة للكافر على المسلم فلا سماع للكافر على المسلم، فصار كأنهما لم يسمعا كلام المسلم ولا شهادة بدون السماع.
ولهما : إن الشهادة إنما شُرِطَتْ في النكاح لِمَا فيه من إثبات ملك المتعة عليها تعظيماً لجزء الآدمي لا لثبوت ملك المهر لها عليه ؛ لأن وجوب المال لا تشترط فيه الشهادة كالبيع ونحوه، ومن المعلوم أن للذمي شهادة على مثله لولايته عليه، فتكفي شهادة الذميين عند كون المرأة ذمية، كذا في «فتح القدير».

As the marriage of a Muslim to a Dhimmī [woman] with two Dhimmī [witnesses] is valid, and it is not made apparent by them if he denies): [This means] the Muslim. For the testimony of a disbeliever against a Muslim is not accepted, and if the Muslim claims [the marriage], it is accepted. [1] His saying: 'as it is valid'; this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad and Zufar disagreed; because there is no testimony of a disbeliever against a Muslim, so there is no hearing for the disbeliever against the Muslim, so it is as if they did not hear the Muslim's statement, and no testimony without hearing. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): Indeed, the condition of testimony in marriage is required for what is in it of establishing the right of legal enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah) over her, as a veneration for the human being's part, not for establishing the right of the dower (mahr) for her against him; because the obligation of wealth does not require testimony in it, like sale and the like. And it is known that the Dhimmī has testimony over his like due to his authority over him, so the testimony of two Dhimmī suffices when the woman is a Dhimmī. This is stated in Fath al-Qadeer. [2] His saying: 'marriage of a Muslim to a Dhimmī'; what is meant by Dhimmī is the Kitābī Dhimmī, for it is permissible for a Muslim to marry her. As for a Dhimmī who is not Jewish or Christian, marriage to her is not permissible for a Muslim. In the same ruling is a Kitābī harbī if a Muslim marries her in Dār al-Harb, even if he dislikes marrying her, as in Sharh Multaqā al-Abhur. [3] His saying: 'with two Dhimmī'; by its generality it indicates that it is contracted in the presence of non-Kitābī as well, like Magians, and that it is not conditional that the religions of the two witnesses and the married woman be in agreement. So if they were Christians and she was Jewish, that suffices. And it excludes harbī, for the testimony of a Harbī against a Dhimmī is not accepted. [4] His saying: 'by them'; and if they became Muslim and then testified, according to both (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is accepted absolutely; but according to Muhammad, it is not accepted, because the contract is not valid unless they say: 'There were two Muslims with us at the contract.' This is in al-Tabyīn. [0] His saying: 'if he denies'; meaning if the husband denies the marriage with the Dhimmī and she claims it against him, the two Dhimmī testify to it. Their testimony is not accepted because the testimony of a disbeliever against a Muslim is not accepted. As for his testimony... (1) Fath al-Qadeer (3:204-205). (2) Tabyīn al-Ḥaqā'iq (2:100).
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a Muslim man's marriage to a Dhimmī woman when the witnesses are also Dhimmī. The matn states that such a marriage is valid, and if the husband denies it, the testimony of the two Dhimmī witnesses is sufficient to establish it. The commentator explains each part of the matn. [1] The ruling that this is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is contrasted with the opinion of Muhammad and Zufar, who argue that testimony of a non-Muslim against a Muslim is never accepted, thus the marriage is invalid because the witnesses are unreliable. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond by distinguishing the purpose of testimony in marriage from financial contracts. In marriage, witnesses are required to establish the right of sexual enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah) due to the dignity of the human being, not for financial rights like dower. Since financial obligations (e.g., sale) do not require witnesses, the analogy with sale fails. Moreover, a Dhimmī has authority (wilāyah) over his fellow Dhimmī, so his testimony is valid concerning her. Thus, two Dhimmī witnesses suffice for a Dhimmī woman. [2] The term 'Dhimmī woman' is clarified: it refers specifically to a Kitābiyya (Jewish or Christian) woman under Muslim protection, as only such women are permissible for Muslim men to marry. A non-Kitābī Dhimmī or a Kitābī Harbī (from enemy territory) is not permissible, though some scholars discuss the latter in certain contexts. [3] The phrase 'with two Dhimmī' is general: it includes non-Kitābī Dhimmīs like Magians, and does not require the witnesses and the woman to share the same religion. For example, two Christian witnesses for a Jewish woman suffice. However, it excludes Harbī men, as their testimony against a Dhimmī is not accepted. [4] A subsidiary issue: if the Dhimmī witnesses later embrace Islam and then testify, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf accept their testimony absolutely, while Muhammad requires them to state that two Muslims were present at the time of the contract. [0] Finally, the case of denial: if the husband denies the marriage and the wife claims it, the testimony of two Dhimmī witnesses is not accepted because a non-Muslim's testimony against a Muslim is not allowed. The passage ends abruptly, but the implication is that such testimony would be rejected, and the wife would need other evidence. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as cited in Fath al-Qadeer.

Key Terms
ذمية (dhimmīyyah): A non-Muslim woman living under Muslim protection (dhimmī) who is a Kitābiyya (Jewish or Christian)
ملك المتعة (milk al-mutʿah): The right to sexual enjoyment in marriage
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony given in court; in marriage, it refers to the presence of witnesses for the contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, a Muslim's marriage to a Dhimmī woman is valid if witnessed by two Dhimmī men. What is the key reason for this ruling?
A) Because financial contracts like sale require witnesses, and marriage is similar.
B) Because the witnesses are of the same faith as the woman and have authority over her, and the condition of witnesses is for the dignity of the marriage, not for financial rights.
C) Because the Muslim husband can choose any witnesses he likes.
D) Because the marriage is only a civil contract and does not require religious witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position is that a Muslim's marriage to a Dhimmī woman is valid with two Dhimmī witnesses, based on the distinction that witnesses in marriage honor human dignity rather than financial rights.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Father's Presence in Agent-Contracted Marriage for Minor Daughter
Ruling on validity depending on father's presence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the validity of a marriage contract conducted by an agent for a minor daughter when the father is present versus absent, and explain the legal reasoning based on witness requirements.

Core Idea
The father's presence completes the witness count; without him, the marriage is invalid.

This passage presents a specific ruling within the broader rules of marriage guardianship. A father may delegate the marriage of his minor daughter to an agent, but the validity of that marriage depends on whether the father is physically present at the contract. The commentary unpacks the legal reasoning, centering on the role of the agent as a mere messenger and the necessity of two witnesses. Understanding this ruling clarifies the intersection of agency and witness requirements in Hanafi Nikah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is a father commanding an agent to marry off his minor daughter. The ruling: valid if the father is present at the contract, invalid if absent.
The matn states the ruling concisely; the commentary elaborates.
2
Identify the Key Condition
The condition for validity is the presence of the father during the marriage contract. This completes the required number of witnesses (two: the father and the one individual present).
Without the father, only one witness exists, which is insufficient.
3
Understand the Legal Reasoning: Transfer of Expression
The agent in marriage is a mere messenger (safīr). When the father is present, his expression transfers to the father, making the father the direct contractor. Thus, the father becomes a witness (by presence) and the agent's expression suffices.
This concept (intiqāl al-'ibārah) is key to Hanafi Nikah agency.
4
Consider the Objections and Responses
Two objections are raised: (1) Why not make the father a witness instead of direct contractor? (2) Why doesn't expression transfer in absence? Responses: Making the father a contractor is forced by the transfer; direct contractor requires physical presence.
This shows the logical consistency of the Hanafi position.
5
Apply to Similar Scenarios
Apply the same condition to any scenario where a father appoints an agent for marriage: presence of the father is necessary if only one other witness is present. If the father is absent, additional witnesses are needed.
This ruling is specific to the case of a minor daughter; similar rules apply for adult daughters consenting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمر آخر أن يُنْكِحَ صغيرته ، فنكح عند فرد إن حضر أبوها صح وإلا فلا (أمر آخر أن يُنْكِحَ صغيرته ، فنكح عند فرد إن حضر أبوها صح وإلا فلا) ؛ فإنَّ الأب إذا كان حاضراً ينتقل عبارة الوكيل إلى الأب ، فصار كأنَّ الأب عاقد له فمقبولة.
[٣]
(۱) قوله : أمر آخر ؛ بنصب الراء مفعول لأمر، وينكح : مضارع معروف من الإنكاح، وضمير صغيرته : راجع الى الأمر ، وحاصله : إن الأب أمر رجلاً أن ينكح بنته الغير البالغة برجل فأنكح ذلك المأمور عند واحد يصح النكاح إن كان الأب حاضراً في ذلك المجلس ؛ لوجود نصاب الشهادة، وإن كان غائباً لا يصح، فإنه إذا كان الأب غائباً، وعقد وكيله النكاح بحضور فردِ لم يوجد نصاب الشهادة، وهو رجلان أو رجل وامرأتان.
[٢] قوله فنكح ؛ الأولى أن يقول : فأنكح ؛ لأنّ الناكح هو الزوج، والمأمور هو المنكح، ومن المناظرين من شدَّد الكاف من التنكيح، وهو خلاف استعمالاتهم. [٣] قوله : ينتقل ...الخ ؛ توضيحه أن الوكيل في النكاح سفير ومعبر محض ينقل عبارة الموكل كما مرَّ سابقاً ، فإذا كان مَن يُعَبِّرُ عنه حاضراً، والغرض أن العبارة تنتقل إليه صار كأنه المباشر للعقد فيكون العاقد هو الأب، ويكون الوكيل المعبر وذلك الفرد شاهدين
بخلاف ما إذا كان الأب غائباً فإن انتقال العبارة إليه حال عدم الحضور لا يجعله مباشراً ؛ لأن المباشر هو مَن يُعَبِّرُ حقيقةً أو حكماً ، وهو حاضر، وحينئذ فيَقْتَصِرُ أثر انتقال العبارة على عدم رجوع الحقوق إلى الوكيل فقط دون جعل الموكل مباشراً، فلا ينعقد النكاح لفقدان الشهادة، وبهذا اندفع إيرادان : الأول : إن الأب في حال حضوره يمكن جعله شاهداً، فأي حاجة إلى تكلف جعله مباشراً، ووجه دفعه أن إنزاله مباشراً عند حضوره جبري لا يتوقف على ثبوت الحاجة إلى اعتباره.
الثاني : إن انتقال العبارة كما أنه في حال الحضور، كذلك في حال عدم الحضور، وجه الفرق بجعله مباشراً عند عدم الحضور، ووجه دفعه : أنّ المباشر يعتبر في مفهومه الحضور فلا يمكن جعله مباشراً حين الغيبوبة.
فما
Another matter: that he commands someone to marry off his minor daughter, and he marries her in front of one individual; if her father is present, it is valid; otherwise not. (Another matter: that he commands someone to marry off his minor daughter, and he marries her in front of one individual; if her father is present, it is valid; otherwise not). For if the father is present, the expression of the agent transfers to the father, so it becomes as if the father himself contracted it, so it is accepted. [3] (1) His saying: 'another matter'; the word 'commander' is accusative as the object of an implied verb, and 'yunkiḥa' is a present tense verb from inkāḥ, and the pronoun 'his minor daughter' refers to the commander. The summary: The father commanded a man to marry his non-pubescent daughter to a man, and that commanded person married her in front of one individual. The marriage is valid if the father was present in that gathering because the required number of witnesses exists; if he was absent, it is not valid. Because if the father is absent, and his agent contracts the marriage in the presence of one individual, the required number of witnesses (two men or one man and two women) does not exist. [2] His saying: 'fa-nakaha'; preferable to say 'fa-ankaha' because the one who marries (nākiḥ) is the husband, while the commanded is the one who gives in marriage (munkiḥ). Some scholars double the kāf from tanzīkīḥ, which is contrary to their usage. [3] His saying: 'transfers...' etc.; explanation: The agent in marriage is a mere messenger and interpreter who conveys the expression of the principal, as previously mentioned. If the one interpreted (the principal) is present, the purpose is that the expression transfers to him, so he becomes as though the direct contractor, making the father the contractor, and the agent is the interpreter, and that one individual [together with the father] [are] two witnesses. Unlike when the father is absent, because the transfer of expression to him in the state of absence does not make him a direct contractor, because the direct contractor is the one who expresses, actually or legally, and he is present. Then the effect of the transfer of expression is limited to rights not reverting to the agent only, without making the principal a direct contractor. So the marriage is not contracted due to loss of witnesses. And with this, two objections are repelled: First: the father, in the state of his presence, could be made a witness; what need is there to bother making him a direct contractor? The rebuttal: making him a direct contractor when he is present is forced, not dependent on proving the need for it. Second: The transfer of expression occurs both in the state of presence and absence; what is the difference? The response: the concept of direct contractor requires presence, so he cannot be made a direct contractor in absence. As for...
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a separate ruling concerning a father who commands an agent to marry off his minor daughter. The matn states: if the father commands someone to marry his minor daughter, and that person performs the marriage in the presence of only one individual, the marriage is valid if the father is present at the time, but invalid if he is absent. The commentator elaborates: [1] The Arabic construction is explained. The agent (al-ma'mūr) is the one who performs the marriage (yunkiḥ). If the father is present in the gathering, the marriage is valid because the required number of witnesses (two males or one male and two females) is fulfilled: the father himself and the one individual present together make two witnesses. If the father is absent, only one witness is present, which is insufficient. [2] A linguistic correction: the verb should be 'fa-ankaha' (he gave in marriage) because the agent is the one who gives in marriage, not the one who marries. [3] The key legal reasoning: The agent in marriage is a mere messenger (safīr) who transmits the expression of the principal (the father). If the principal is present, the expression transfers to him, making him the actual contractor. Thus, the father becomes the direct party, and the agent acts as a mere interpreter; together with the one witness, they constitute two witnesses. If the father is absent, the expression cannot transfer to him because the concept of being a direct contractor requires physical presence. Therefore, the agent remains the one who expresses, and the witness count is only one, invalidating the marriage. Two objections are raised and answered: First, why not simply make the father a witness instead of a contractor? The answer: when the father is present, making him the contractor is forced by the transfer of expression; it is not a matter of choice. Second, if expression transfers in presence, why not in absence? The answer: the direct contractor must be present; an absent person cannot be considered a direct contractor. Thus, the ruling is clear: if the father is in attendance, the marriage is valid; otherwise, it is not.

Key Terms
تنكيح (tankīḥ): The act of giving a woman in marriage by her guardian
وكيل (wakīl): Agent appointed to conduct the marriage
سفير (safīr): Messenger or interpreter who conveys the contractual expression without personal authority

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father orders an agent to marry his minor daughter, and the agent performs the marriage in the presence of only one witness while the father is absent, what is the ruling?
A) The marriage is valid because the agent acts on behalf of the father.
B) The marriage is invalid because the required number of witnesses is not met.
C) The marriage is valid if the witness is a Muslim man.
D) The marriage is valid only if the daughter is present.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an agent-contracted marriage for a minor daughter hinges on the father's presence to complete the witness requirement; without him, the marriage is void.

Marriage with One Witness: The Principle of Transfer of Expression
Commentary on a ruling about insufficient witnesses in marriage agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage with one witness when the principal is present, and apply the principle of intiqal al-ibarah to determine validity.

Core Idea
When the principal is present, the agent's words transfer to them, making them the direct contractor and completing the witness requirement.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a specific scenario in marriage contracts: an agent contracts marriage with only one witness. The ruling hinges on whether the principal (the father for a minor daughter, or the adult daughter herself) is present. The commentary introduces the principle of transfer of expression (intiqal al-ibarah) to resolve the witness deficiency, a uniquely Hanafi approach that underscores the school's pragmatic flexibility in contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the main case: a father orders an agent to marry his minor daughter. The agent performs the contract in the presence of only one male witness.
The standard requirement for marriage witnesses is two men or one man and two women (Hanafi view).
2
Analyze the Ruling with Principal Present
If the father (principal) is present at the contract, the marriage is valid. The agent's words are legally transferred to the father (intiqal al-ibarah), making him the direct contractor. Thus, the father and the agent together serve as two witnesses.
This transfer occurs because the agent is a mere conveyor; the principal's presence allows the expression to attach to him.
3
Contrast with Principal Absent
If the father is absent, the agent remains the direct contractor, and with only one witness, the witness requirement is not met. The marriage is invalid.
The transfer of expression does not make the absent principal a direct contractor because direct contracting requires physical presence.
4
Apply to Analogous Case: Adult Daughter
The same principle applies when a father marries his adult daughter with one witness. If the daughter is present, the marriage is valid because the father's expression transfers to her, making her the contracting party. The father and the witness serve as two witnesses.
The adult daughter's consent is also required, but here the focus is on the witness issue.
5
Address Potential Objections
The commentary addresses two objections: (1) Why not just make the father a witness? The answer: making him the direct contractor is necessary. (2) Since transfer occurs even when absent, why not validate? The answer: direct contractor must be present.
This reasoning solidifies the rule and shows legal craftsmanship.

أمر آخر أن يُنْكِحَ صغيرته ، فنكح عند فرد إن حضر أبوها صح وإلا فلا) ؛ فإنَّ الأب إذا كان حاضراً ينتقل عبارة الوكيل إلى الأب ، فصار كأنَّ الأب عاقد له فمقبولة. [١] قوله : أمر آخر ؛ بنصب الراء مفعول لأمر، وينكح : مضارع معروف من الإنكاح، وضمير صغيرته : راجع الى الأمر ، وحاصله : إن الأب أمر رجلاً أن ينكح بنته الغير البالغة برجل فأنكح ذلك المأمور عند واحد يصح النكاح إن كان الأب حاضراً في ذلك المجلس ؛ لوجود نصاب الشهادة، وإن كان غائباً لا يصح، فإنه إذا كان الأب غائباً، وعقد وكيله النكاح بحضور فردِ لم يوجد نصاب الشهادة، وهو رجلان أو رجل وامرأتان. [٢] قوله فنكح ؛ الأولى أن يقول : فأنكح ؛ لأنّ الناكح هو الزوج، والمأمور هو المنكح، ومن المناظرين من شدَّد الكاف من التنكيح، وهو خلاف استعمالاتهم. [٣] قوله : ينتقل ...الخ ؛ توضيحه أن الوكيل في النكاح سفير ومعبر محض ينقل عبارة الموكل كما مرَّ سابقاً ، فإذا كان مَن يُعَبِّرُ عنه حاضراً، والغرض أن العبارة تنتقل إليه صار كأنه المباشر للعقد فيكون العاقد هو الأب، ويكون الوكيل المعبر وذلك الفرد شاهدين بخلاف ما إذا كان الأب غائباً فإن انتقال العبارة إليه حال عدم الحضور لا يجعله مباشراً ؛ لأن المباشر هو مَن يُعَبِّرُ حقيقةً أو حكماً ، وهو حاضر، وحينئذ فيَقْتَصِرُ أثر انتقال العبارة على عدم رجوع الحقوق إلى الوكيل فقط دون جعل الموكل مباشراً، فلا ينعقد النكاح لفقدان الشهادة، وبهذا اندفع إيرادان : الأول : إن الأب في حال حضوره يمكن جعله شاهداً، فأي حاجة إلى تكلف جعله مباشراً، ووجه دفعه أن إنزاله مباشراً عند حضوره جبري لا يتوقف على ثبوت الحاجة إلى اعتباره. الثاني : إن انتقال العبارة كما أنه في حال الحضور، كذلك في حال عدم الحضور، وجه الفرق بجعله مباشراً عند عدم الحضور، ووجه دفعه : أنّ المباشر يعتبر في مفهومه الحضور فلا يمكن جعله مباشراً حين الغيبوبة. فما كأب يُنْكِحُ بالغته عند فرد إن حضرت صح وإلا فلا)، فصار كأنَّ البالغة عاقدة ، والأب وذلك الفرد شاهدان، وعبارة «المختصر» هذا : والوكيل شاهد إن حضر موكَّلُهُ كالولي إن حضرَتْ مُولِيَّتُهُ بالغة (١). [١]قوله : شاهدان ؛ لكن شهادة المأمور إنما تقبل عند الإثبات إذا لم يذكر أنه عقده ، وإلا لا تقبل ؛ لكونه شهادة على فعل نفسه. (٢) قوله : صح؛ كما يصح نكاح العبد إذا أذن له المولى، فعقد بحضرة المولى ورجل، ولو زَوَّجَ المولى عبده البالغ بحضرة العبد وواحد لم يجز، كذا في «الفتح». [٣] قوله : عاقدة ؛ لانتقال عبارة الأب إليها، فتصير مباشرة حكماً، ولا كذلك إذا لم تكن حاضرة في المجلس. [٤] قوله : موليته ؛ أي المرأة التي يلي الولي أمرها، وهو اسم مفعول من وَلَى الأمر إذا تولاه. (١) انتهى من النقاية»(ص٧٤).
Another matter: that he commands (someone) to marry off his minor daughter, so he (the commanded) marries (her) in the presence of one (witness). If her father is present, it is valid; otherwise not. Because when the father is present, the expression of the agent transfers to the father, so it is as if the father is the contracting party, so it is accepted. [1] His saying: 'Another matter' — with the accusative of the letter ra, it is the object of 'command', and 'yunkihu' is a present tense verb meaning to give in marriage, and the pronoun of 'his minor daughter' refers to the commander. The summary: that the father commanded a man to marry his non-adult daughter to a man, and that commanded one married her in the presence of one (witness). The marriage is valid if the father was present in that gathering, because the required number of witnesses is met; if he was absent, it is not valid. Because when the father is absent, and his deputy contracts the marriage in the presence of one individual, the required number of witnesses, which is two men or one man and two women, is not found. [2] His saying: 'so he married' — it would be better to say: 'so he gave in marriage', because the one who marries (naakih) is the husband, and the commanded one is the one who gives in marriage (munkih). Some debaters emphasize the kaf from 'tankiih', which is contrary to their usage. [3] His saying: 'transfers...etc.' — its clarification: that the agent in marriage is a mere intermediary and conveyor who transmits the expression of the principal, as previously mentioned. So if the one for whom the expression is conveyed is present, and the purpose is that the expression transfers to him, he becomes as if he is the direct contracting party, so the contracting party is the father, and the agent becomes the conveyor and that individual (is) two witnesses. Contrary to when the father is absent, because the transfer of expression to him during absence does not make him a direct contractor; because the direct contractor is the one who actually or constructively conveys, and he is present. In that case, the effect of the transfer of expression is limited to the rights not reverting to the agent only, without making the principal a direct contractor. So the marriage is not concluded due to lack of witnesses. And by this, two objections are repelled: First: that the father in his presence could be made a witness, so what is the need to burden making him a direct contractor? The repelling of it: that making him a direct contractor when present is forced and does not depend on establishing a need for considering him. Second: that the transfer of expression, as it occurs in presence, also occurs in absence. The difference is by making him a direct contractor in absence, repelling it: that the direct contractor requires presence in its concept, so he cannot be made a direct contractor during absence. So [the analogy] is like a father who marries his adult daughter in the presence of one (witness): if she is present, it is valid; otherwise no. So it is as if the adult daughter is the contracting party, and the father and that individual are two witnesses. And the expression in 'al-Mukhtasar' is: 'And the agent is a witness if his principal is present, like the guardian if his ward (an adult female) is present.' [1] His saying: 'two witnesses' — but the testimony of the commanded one is only accepted in establishing (the contract) if he does not mention that he contracted it; otherwise it is not accepted, because it would be bearing witness concerning his own action. (2) His saying: 'valid' — just as the marriage of a slave is valid if the master permits him, and he contracts in the presence of the master and one man. If the master married off his adult slave in the presence of the slave and one (witness), it is not permissible. As in 'al-Fath'. [3] His saying: 'contracting party' — because the expression of the father transfers to her, so she becomes a direct contractor constructively, and not so if she is not present in the gathering. [4] His saying: 'his ward' (mawliya) — that is, the woman whose affairs the guardian manages; it is a passive participle from 'waliya al-amra' when he undertakes it. (1) End from al-Niqayah (p.74).
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explaining a ruling from al-Wiqayah regarding marriage with insufficient witnesses. The main scenario: a father orders someone to act as his agent to marry off his minor daughter. The agent contracts the marriage in the presence of only one male witness (instead of the required two). The ruling is that the marriage is valid if the father is present at the contract; otherwise it is invalid. The reason given is the Hanafi principle of 'intiqal al-ibarah' (transfer of expression): when the principal (father) is present, the agent's words are legally attributed to the father, making him the direct contracting party. Consequently, the father and the agent together constitute two witnesses, fulfilling the requirement. If the father is absent, the agent remains the direct contractor, and with only one witness present, the marriage is void. The commentator then addresses two objections: (1) Why not simply make the father a witness? The response is that making him a direct contractor is a necessary legal construction when present. (2) Since the transfer of expression occurs even when absent, why not make him a direct contractor then? The response is that the concept of a direct contractor requires physical presence. The passage then draws an analogy with the case of an adult daughter: if a father marries off his adult daughter by saying 'I give her in marriage' in the presence of one witness, the marriage is valid if the daughter is present, because she becomes the direct contracting party via transfer of expression from the father, with the father and the witness serving as two witnesses. The commentator also includes a cross-reference to the slave case: a slave's marriage with the master's permission and the master's presence is valid with one additional witness, but if the master directly marries off the slave in the slave's presence with only one witness, it is invalid.

Key Terms
صغيرته (saghiratihi): His minor daughter who has not reached puberty and is under the father's guardianship for marriage.
بالغته (balighatihi): His adult daughter who has reached puberty and has legal capacity to consent to marriage.
عند فرد (inda fard): In the presence of only one male witness, insufficient for the standard requirement of two men or one man and two women.
ينتقل عبارة (yantaqil 'ibarah): The legal transfer of the agent's words to the principal, making the principal the direct contracting party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father orders an agent to marry his minor daughter, and the agent contracts the marriage in the presence of only one male witness, when is the marriage valid?
A) Always valid because the father's order suffices.
B) Valid only if the father is present at the contract.
C) Valid only if the daughter is present.
D) Never valid because two witnesses are always required.

Key Takeaway
The marriage is valid when the principal is present because transfer of expression fulfills the witness requirement.

Ruling on Father Marrying His Mature Daughter with Witness
Ruling with commentary on condition of woman's presence in marriage by father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid marriage when a father acts as agent for his mature daughter and explain the legal effect of her presence.

Core Idea
A father can marry his mature daughter with one witness, but only if she is present in the council.

This passage from the chapter on marriage clarifies a specific scenario: a father acting as agent to marry his mature daughter. The key issue is whether the daughter's presence is required. The ruling distinguishes between valid and invalid based on her attendance, and the commentary explains the underlying legal reasoning, including an analogy with a slave's marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The father marries his mature daughter with only one witness (in addition to himself). State the ruling: valid if the daughter is present, invalid otherwise.
The matn uses 'إن حضرت صح وإلا فلا'.
2
Unpack the condition of presence
Understand why her presence matters: she becomes the direct contracting party (al-āqidah), with the father and the witness serving as two witnesses. This is explained by the transfer of speech ('ibārat al-ab) to her.
Refer to the commentary on 'عاقدة'.
3
Analyze the analogy with a slave's marriage
The commentary compares two cases: (a) master permits slave to marry, slave contracts in master's presence with one witness – valid; (b) master marries the slave in the slave's presence with one witness – invalid. Extract the principle: the presence of the principal (slave/daughter) transforms the agent's role into a witness.
The invalidity in (b) occurs because the slave is not the contracting party; the master acts as agent but the slave's presence alone does not make him a contracting party.
4
Note the evidentiary issue for the agent
The commentary warns that if the agent later testifies about the marriage, his testimony is not accepted because it would be about his own act. This is a practical consequence.
This does not affect validity, only future litigation.

Source Text

كأب يُنْكِحُ بالغته عند فرد إن حضرت صح وإلا فلا
والوكيل مع ذلك الفردِ شاهدان ، كأَبِ يُنْكِحُ بالغتَهُ عند فردٍ إن حضرت صح وإلا فلا)، فصار كأنَّ البالغة عاقدة ، والأب وذلك الفرد شاهدان، وعبارة «المختصر» هذا : والوكيل شاهد إن حضر موكَّلُهُ كالولي إن حضرَتْ مُولِيَّتُهُ ) بالغة (١).
[١]قوله : شاهدان ؛ لكن شهادة المأمور إنما تقبل عند الإثبات إذا لم يذكر أنه عقده ، وإلا لا تقبل ؛ لكونه شهادة على فعل نفسه. (۲) قوله : صح؛ كما يصح نكاح العبد إذا أذن له المولى، فعقد بحضرة المولى ورجل، ولو زَوَّجَ المولى عبده البالغ بحضرة العبد وواحد لم يجز، كذا في «الفتح». [٣] قوله : عاقدة ؛ لانتقال عبارة الأب إليها، فتصير مباشرة حكماً، ولا كذلك إذا لم تكن حاضرة في المجلس.
[٤] قوله : موليته ؛ أي المرأة التي يلي الولي أمرها، وهو اسم مفعول من وَلَى الأمر إذا تولاه.
(۱) انتهى من النقاية»(ص٧٤).
Like a father who marries his mature daughter in the presence of an individual; if she is present, it is valid, otherwise not. And the agent along with that individual are two witnesses, as a father who marries his mature daughter in the presence of an individual; if she is present, it is valid, otherwise not. Thus it becomes as if the mature woman is the contracting party, and the father and that individual are two witnesses. The expression of al-Mukhtasar is: The agent is a witness if his principal is present, like the guardian if his ward (the woman) is present. [1] He said: two witnesses; but the testimony of the appointed person is only accepted in litigation if he does not mention that he was the one who contracted it, otherwise it is not accepted because it would be testimony about his own act. [2] He said: it is valid; just as the marriage of a slave is valid when the master permits him, and he contracts in the presence of the master and a man. But if the master marries his adult slave in the presence of the slave and one person, it is not valid, as in al-Fath. [3] He said: contracting party; because the father's speech transfers to her, so she becomes the direct party effectively, unlike when she is not present in the council. [4] He said: his ward; meaning the woman whose affairs the guardian manages; it is a passive participle from waliya al-amr (to undertake the matter). (1) End from al-Nuqayah (p. 74).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the main text (matn) of the chapter on marriage. The passage explains the validity condition when a father acts as agent to marry his mature daughter. The key condition is the presence of the daughter in the marriage council. If she is present, the marriage is valid; if absent, it is invalid. The commentary elaborates that in such a case, the father and the single witness serve as two witnesses, and effectively the daughter becomes the direct contracting party (because the father's speech is considered to transfer to her). The commentary also draws an analogy with a slave's marriage: if the master permits the slave to marry, the presence of the master and one witness makes it valid; but if the master himself contracts the marriage for his adult slave in the presence of the slave and only one witness, it is invalid—indicating that the slave's presence alone does not substitute for his own consent as a contracting party. The passage also clarifies that the agent's testimony is problematic if he testifies to a contract he himself executed. This ruling is drawn from Zahir al-Riwayah sources as reflected in al-Hidayah and its commentaries.

Key Terms
فرد (fard): Here it means a single individual, specifically a witness.
ولي (wali): Guardian who has the authority to contract marriage for a woman.
عاقدة (āqidah): The woman who is the actual contracting party in the marriage when the father acts as her agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father marries his mature daughter without her being present but with one witness, is the marriage valid according to this passage?
A) Yes, because the father has authority.
B) No, because her presence is required.
C) Yes, if she gives consent later.
D) No, because two witnesses are needed.

Key Takeaway
A father marrying his mature daughter with one witness is valid only if she is present in the marriage council, as her presence makes her the direct contracting party and completes the witness requirement.

Categories of Permanently Prohibited Women in Marriage
Opening of chapter listing prohibited women with Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven categories of permanently prohibited women in marriage according to the Hanafi school and identify the Quranic proof for each.

Core Idea
Seven categories of women are permanently prohibited from marriage, based on Quranic text.

This passage opens the chapter on prohibited women (muharramat) in marriage. The matn lists the core categories based on blood, affinity, and fosterage, while the commentary expands to seven comprehensive types. Understanding these categories is essential for determining valid marriage in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core list
Read the matn: 'It is prohibited for a man: his origin, his branch, his sister, her daughter, his brother's daughter, his paternal aunt, his maternal aunt, and his wife's daughter if he has consummated.' Note each relation.
2
Classify the seven types
From the commentary, list the seven categories: nasab, sihr, rada', jam', haqq al-ghayr, lack of revealed religion, and tanafi. Match each item in the matn to its category.
Sihr has four subtypes explained elsewhere.
3
Locate the Quranic proof
The author quotes Surah an-Nisa 4:23-24. Identify which phrase prohibits each category. For example, 'your mothers' prohibits the origin.
The verse is qati al-thubut and qati al-dalala.
4
Note the condition for stepdaughter
The prohibition of a stepdaughter applies only if the marriage to her mother was consummated. Find the corresponding condition in the verse: 'if you have entered with them'
This is an important exception.
5
Apply the categories to a scenario
Given a hypothetical family tree, determine which women are permanently prohibited and why. Use the seven categories as a checklist.
Always start with the verse.

Chapter of Prohibited Women. And it is prohibited for a man: his origin (mother/grandmother), his branch (daughter/granddaughter), his sister, her daughter, his brother's daughter, his paternal aunt, his maternal aunt, and his wife's daughter if he has consummated the marriage. Chapter of Prohibited Women. [1] Types: The text. [1] He said: 'It is prohibited for a man...' etc. It is said in Kashf al-Wiqaya: Know that prohibited women are of types: The first: those prohibited by lineage. The second type: those prohibited by affinity (sihr), and it has four subtypes. The third type: those prohibited by fosterage. The fourth type: prohibition of combining (two sisters in marriage). The fifth type: prohibition due to the right of another, such as another's wife or a woman in her waiting period. The sixth type: prohibited due to lacking a revealed religion. The seventh type: prohibited due to incompatibility, such as a mistress marrying her slave. The details of each type will follow. [2] He said: if consummated; the basis for the prohibition of these mentioned is His statement: 'Forbidden to you are your mothers, your daughters, your sisters, your paternal aunts, your maternal aunts, your brother's daughters, your sister's daughters, your mothers who nursed you, your sisters from fosterage, your wives' mothers, your stepdaughters under your guardianship from the wives you have consummated with, but if you have not consummated, there is no sin on you, and the wives of your sons from your loins, and to combine two sisters except what has passed. Indeed, Allah is Forgiving, Merciful.' And married women except those your right hands possess. (1) An-Nisa: 23-24.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on permanently prohibited women (muharramat) in marriage. The matn lists the primary categories: ancestors (mother, grandmother), descendants (daughter, granddaughters), siblings (sister), their children (sister's daughter, brother's daughter), aunts (paternal and maternal), and stepdaughter if the marriage with her mother has been consummated. The commentary from Kashf al-Wiqaya organizes the prohibitions into seven comprehensive types: 1) Blood relations (nasab), 2) Affinity through marriage (sihr) with four subcategories, 3) Fosterage (rada'), 4) Prohibition of combining two women related by blood or marriage (e.g., two sisters), 5) Prohibition due to the rights of others (e.g., another man's wife or a woman in her 'iddah), 6) Prohibition due to lack of a revealed religion (i.e., a Muslim cannot marry a polytheist), and 7) Prohibition due to incompatibility (e.g., a female master marrying her own slave). The primary proof is the Quranic passage from Surah an-Nisa (4:23-24), which is quoted as the foundational text for these prohibitions. The phrase 'if consummated' (in wuti'at) refers to the condition that marriage to a stepdaughter is prohibited only if the marriage to her mother has been consummated; otherwise, it is permissible. This is a classic Hanafi presentation: the ruling is stated, then categorized systematically, and supported by explicit Quranic evidence.

Key Terms
محرمات (muḥarramāt): Women who are permanently or temporarily prohibited for marriage.
نسب (nasab): Blood lineage; the first category of prohibited women based on consanguinity.
مصاهرة (muṣāharah): Affinity; relationship established through marriage, leading to prohibitions.
رضاع (raḍāʿ): Fosterage; the third category, where breastfeeding establishes a prohibition similar to blood relations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of prohibition is not mentioned in the initial list (nasab, sihr, etc.) but is included in Kashf al-Wiqaya's seven types?
A) Prohibition by fosterage
B) Prohibition due to incompatibility (tanafi)
C) Prohibition of combining two sisters
D) Prohibition due to lack of revealed religion

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school identifies seven categories of permanently prohibited women, all grounded in the explicit Quranic text of Surah an-Nisa 4:23-24.

The Seven Categories of Prohibited Women in Marriage
Opening the chapter on prohibited women with Quranic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven categories of women permanently prohibited for marriage in Hanafi fiqh and explain the Quranic evidence and linguistic expansions for these prohibitions.

Core Idea
The Quran explicitly forbids marriage with seven categories of women; these prohibitions are permanent and extend to all relatives within those categories.

This passage opens the chapter on prohibited women (al-muharramat) by categorizing them into seven distinct types and grounding them in the explicit text of the Quran. Understanding these categories is essential for any student of Hanafi fiqh, as they define the unbreachable boundaries of permissible marriage. The commentary expands the explicit terms through linguistic principles, showing that 'mothers' includes grandmothers, 'daughters' includes all female descendants, and 'aunts' includes those of one's parents and grandparents.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the Seven Categories
List the seven types of prohibited women as given in the text: 1) by lineage, 2) by affinity (four subtypes), 3) by fosterage, 4) prohibition of combining two sisters, 5) due to the right of another (married or waiting women), 6) due to lack of a revealed religion, and 7) due to incompatibility (master-slave marriage).
The first three categories are primary and are detailed in the Quran; the others are derived from additional evidence.
2
Identify the Primary Proof
Locate the Quranic verse cited: Surah al-Nisa' 4:23-24, and also 4:22 about marrying what one's father married. Recognize that this is the foundational evidence for the prohibition.
The verse uses the word 'حُرِّمَتْ' which indicates an absolute prohibition, not a mere discouragement.
3
Analyze Linguistic Expansions
Understand how the commentary expands the explicit terms: 'mothers' includes all ascendant females (grandmothers, etc.), 'daughters' includes all descendant females (granddaughters, etc.), and 'aunts' includes those of one's parents and grandparents. This is done by the principle that the literal meaning of these terms extends to all individuals covered by the definition.
This expansion is by consensus and is a standard method in Hanafi usul for interpreting general terms.
4
Apply the Principle of Conditionality
Note that the prohibition of step-daughters (ربائب) is conditional upon having consummated the marriage with their mother. This condition is derived from the verse 'فإن لم تكونوا دخلتم بهن فلا جناح عليكم' — if you have not entered with them, there is no sin.
This condition applies only to step-daughters; other prohibitions are unconditional.
5
Recognize Analogous Prohibitions for Women
Understand that the same prohibitions apply to women analogously, with some gender-specific differences. For example, a woman is forbidden to marry her father's brother (uncle) just as a man is forbidden to marry his paternal aunt.
The text states 'يؤخذ في جانب النساء نظير ما يؤخذ في جانب الرجل' — the equivalent is taken for women.

Source Text
